<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="32.392222222222" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Spitfire_fly_past_at_RAF_Halton.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="95875" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (96 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e5/Spitfire_fly_past_at_RAF_Halton.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'></source></audio></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_fly_past_at_RAF_Halton.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Audio recording of Spitfire fly past at the 2011 family day at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Halton" title="RAF Halton">RAF Halton</a>, Buckinghamshire.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <b>Supermarine Spitfire</b> is a British single-seat <a href="/wiki/Fighter_aircraft" title="Fighter aircraft">fighter aircraft</a> that was used by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> and many other <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> countries before, during and after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The Spitfire was built in many variants, using several wing configurations, and was produced in greater numbers than any other British aircraft. It was also the only British fighter to be in continuous production throughout the war. The Spitfire continues to be popular among enthusiasts; about <a href="/wiki/List_of_surviving_Supermarine_Spitfires" title="List of surviving Supermarine Spitfires">54 remain airworthy</a>, while many more are static exhibits in aviation museums throughout the world.</p>
<p>The Spitfire was designed as a short-range, high-performance <a href="/wiki/Interceptor_aircraft" title="Interceptor aircraft">interceptor aircraft</a> by <a href="/wiki/R._J._Mitchell" title="R. J. Mitchell">R. J. Mitchell</a>, chief designer at <a href="/wiki/Supermarine" title="Supermarine">Supermarine Aviation Works</a>, which operated as a subsidiary of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vickers-Armstrong" title="Vickers-Armstrong">Vickers-Armstrong</a> from 1928. Mitchell pushed the Spitfire's distinctive <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_wing" title="Elliptical wing">elliptical wing</a> (designed by B. Shenstone) to have the thinnest possible cross-section, helping give the aircraft a higher top speed than several contemporary fighters, including the <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Hurricane" title="Hawker Hurricane">Hawker Hurricane</a>. Mitchell continued to refine the design until his death in 1937, whereupon his colleague <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith_(aircraft_designer)" title="Joseph Smith (aircraft designer)">Joseph Smith</a> took over as chief designer, overseeing the development of the Spitfire through its multitude of variants.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a>, from July to October 1940, the Spitfire was perceived by the public to be the main RAF fighter, though the more numerous Hawker Hurricane shouldered a greater proportion of the burden against the Nazi German air force, the <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a>. Spitfire units, however, had a lower attrition rate and a higher victory-to-loss ratio than those flying Hurricanes because of its higher performance. Spitfires in general were tasked with engaging Luftwaffe fighters (mainly <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109_variants#Bf_109E" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109 variants">Messerschmitt Bf 109E</a> series aircraft which were a close match for the Spitfire) during the Battle.</p>
<p>After the Battle of Britain, the Spitfire superseded the Hurricane to become the backbone of <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fighter_Command" title="RAF Fighter Command">RAF Fighter Command</a>, and saw action in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Theatre_of_World_War_II" title="European Theatre of World War II">European</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean,_Middle_East_and_African_theatres_of_World_War_II" title="Mediterranean, Middle East and African theatres of World War II">Mediterranean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asiatic-Pacific_Theater" title="Asiatic-Pacific Theater">Pacific</a> and the <a href="/wiki/South-East_Asian_theatre_of_World_War_II" title="South-East Asian theatre of World War II">South-East Asian</a> theatres. Much loved by its pilots, the Spitfire served in several roles, including interceptor, photo-reconnaissance, fighter-bomber and trainer, and it continued to serve in these roles until the 1950s. The <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Seafire" title="Supermarine Seafire">Seafire</a> was a carrier-based adaptation of the Spitfire which served in the <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a> from 1942 through to the mid-1950s. Although the original airframe was designed to be powered by a <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">Rolls-Royce Merlin</a> engine producing 1,030 <a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">hp</a> (768 kW), it was strong enough and adaptable enough to use increasingly powerful Merlins and, in later marks, <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Griffon" title="Rolls-Royce Griffon">Rolls-Royce Griffon</a> engines producing up to 2,340 hp (1,745 kW); as a consequence of this the <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_variants:_specifications,_performance_and_armament" title="Supermarine Spitfire variants: specifications, performance and armament">Spitfire's performance and capabilities</a> improved over the course of its life.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_and_production">Development and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/R._J._Mitchell" title="R. J. Mitchell">R. J. Mitchell</a>'s 1931 design to meet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_Of_Air_Ministry_Specifications" title="List Of Air Ministry Specifications">Air Ministry specification F7/30</a> for a new and modern fighter capable of 250 mph (400 km/h), the <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Type_224" title="Supermarine Type 224">Supermarine Type 224</a>, was an open-cockpit monoplane with bulky gull-wings and a large fixed, spatted <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">undercarriage</a> powered by the 600 horsepower (450 kW) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evaporative_cooling_(engine)" title="Evaporative cooling (engine)">evaporatively cooled</a> <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Goshawk" title="Rolls-Royce Goshawk">Rolls-Royce Goshawk</a> engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> This made its first flight in February 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews_Supermarine.2C_p._206_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews_Supermarine.2C_p._206-7">[6]</a></sup> Of the seven designs tendered to F7/30, the <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Gladiator" title="Gloster Gladiator">Gloster Gladiator</a> biplane was accepted for service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-document_p._16_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-document_p._16-8">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Type 224 was a big disappointment to Mitchell and his design team, who immediately embarked on a series of "cleaned-up" designs, using their experience with the <a href="/wiki/Schneider_Trophy" title="Schneider Trophy">Schneider Trophy</a> seaplanes as a starting point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-document_p._16_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-document_p._16-8">[7]</a></sup> This led to the Type 300, with retractable undercarriage and the wingspan reduced by 6 ft (1.8 m). This was submitted to the Air Ministry in July 1934, but was not accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> The design then went through a series of changes, including the incorporation of a faired, enclosed cockpit, oxygen-breathing apparatus, smaller and thinner wings, and the newly developed, more powerful Rolls-Royce <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">PV-XII</a> V-12 engine, later named the "Merlin". In November 1934 Mitchell, with the backing of Supermarine's owner, Vickers-Armstrong, started detailed design work on this refined version of the Type 300<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 December 1934, the Air Ministry issued contract <i>AM 361140/34</i>, providing £10,000 for the construction of Mitchell's improved F7/30 design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-document_p._20_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-document_p._20-11">[10]</a></sup> On 3 January 1935, they formalised the contract with a new specification, F10/35, written around the aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> In April 1935, the armament was changed from two .303 in (7.7 mm) <a href="/wiki/Vickers_machine_gun" title="Vickers machine gun">Vickers machine guns</a> in each wing to four .303 in (7.7 mm) <a href="/wiki/M1919_Browning_machine_gun" title="M1919 Browning machine gun">Brownings</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-document_p._32_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-document_p._32-13">[12]</a></sup> following a recommendation by Squadron Leader <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Sorley" title="Ralph Sorley">Ralph Sorley</a> of the Operational Requirements section at the Air Ministry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_2006.2C_pp._37.E2.80.9338_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_2006.2C_pp._37.E2.80.9338-14">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 5 March 1936,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[nb 2]</a></sup> the prototype (<i>K5054</i>) took off on its first flight from <a href="/wiki/Southampton_Airport" title="Southampton Airport">Eastleigh Aerodrome</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a> Airport). At the controls was <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Summers" title="Joseph Summers">Captain Joseph "Mutt" Summers</a>, chief test pilot for Vickers, who is quoted as saying "Don't touch anything" on landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[nb 3]</a></sup> This eight-minute flight<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_2006.2C_pp._37.E2.80.9338_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_2006.2C_pp._37.E2.80.9338-14">[13]</a></sup> came four months after the maiden flight of the contemporary Hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>K5054</i> was fitted with a new propeller, and Summers flew the aircraft on 10 March 1936; during this flight the undercarriage was retracted for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup> After the fourth flight, a new engine was fitted, and Summers left the test-flying to his assistants, <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Quill" title="Jeffrey Quill">Jeffrey Quill</a> and George Pickering. They soon discovered that the Spitfire<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[nb 4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._55._24-1"><a href="#cite_note-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._55.-24">[21]</a></sup> was a very good aircraft, but not perfect. The rudder was over-sensitive and the top speed was just 330 mph (528 km/h), little faster than <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Camm" title="Sydney Camm">Sydney Camm</a>'s new Merlin-powered Hurricane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[23]</a></sup> A new and better-shaped wooden propeller allowed the Spitfire to reach 348 mph (557 km/h) in level flight in mid-May, when Summers flew <i>K5054</i> to <a href="/wiki/RAF_Martlesham_Heath" title="RAF Martlesham Heath">RAF Martlesham Heath</a> and handed the aircraft over to Squadron Leader Anderson of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeroplane_%26_Armament_Experimental_Establishment" title="Aeroplane &amp; Armament Experimental Establishment">Aeroplane &amp; Armament Experimental Establishment</a> (A&amp;AEE). Here, Flight Lieutenant Humphrey Edwardes-Jones took over the prototype for the RAF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup> He had been given orders to fly the aircraft and then to make his report to the Air Ministry on landing. Edwardes-Jones's report was positive; his only request was that the Spitfire be equipped with an undercarriage position indicator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup> A week later, on 3 June 1936, the Air Ministry placed an order for 310 Spitfires,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup> before any formal report had been issued by the A&amp;AEE; interim reports were later issued on a piecemeal basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-price_document.2C_p._25_9_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-price_document.2C_p._25_9-31">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_production">Initial production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Initial production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The British public first saw the Spitfire at the <a href="/wiki/Hendon_Aerodrome" title="Hendon Aerodrome">RAF Hendon</a> air-display on Saturday 27 June 1936. Although full-scale production was supposed to begin immediately, there were numerous problems that could not be overcome for some time and the first production Spitfire, <i>K9787</i>, did not roll off the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woolston,_Hampshire" title="Woolston, Hampshire">Woolston</a>, Southampton assembly line until mid-1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethell_p._12_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ethell_p._12-1">[1]</a></sup> The first and most immediate problem was that the main Supermarine factory at Woolston was already working at full capacity fulfilling orders for <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Walrus" title="Supermarine Walrus">Walrus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Stranraer" title="Supermarine Stranraer">Stranraer</a> flying boats. Although outside contractors were supposed to be involved in manufacturing many important Spitfire components, especially the wings, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vickers-Armstrong" title="Vickers-Armstrong">Vickers-Armstrong</a> (the parent company) was reluctant to see the Spitfire being manufactured by outside concerns and was slow to release the necessary blueprints and subcomponents. As a result of the delays in getting the Spitfire into full production, the Air Ministry put forward a plan that production of the Spitfire be stopped after the initial order for 310, after which Supermarine would build <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Beaufighter" title="Bristol Beaufighter">Bristol Beaufighters</a>. The managements of Supermarine and Vickers were able to convince the Air Ministry that the problems could be overcome and further orders were placed for 200 Spitfires on 24 March 1938, the two orders covering the K, L and N prefix serial numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 1936 the director of Vickers-Armstrongs, Sir Robert MacLean, guaranteed production of five aircraft a week, beginning 15 months after an order was placed. On 3 June 1936, the Air Ministry placed an order for 310 aircraft, for a price of <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">£</a>1,395,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._61_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._61-33">[29]</a></sup> Full-scale production of the Spitfire began at Supermarine's facility in Woolston, Southampton, but it quickly became clear that the order could not be completed in the 15 months promised. Supermarine was a small company, already busy building Walrus and Stranraer flying boats, and Vickers was busy building the <a href="/wiki/Vickers_Wellington" title="Vickers Wellington">Wellingtons</a>. The initial solution was to subcontract the work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._61_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._61-33">[29]</a></sup> The first production Spitfire rolled off the assembly line in mid-1938,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethell_p._12_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ethell_p._12-1">[1]</a></sup> and was flown by Jeffrey Quill on 15 May 1938, almost 24 months after the initial order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The final cost of the first 310 aircraft, after delays and increased programme costs, came to £1,870,242 or £1,533 more per aircraft than originally estimated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._67_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._67-4">[4]</a></sup> Production aircraft cost about £9,500. The most expensive components were the hand-fabricated and finished fuselage at approximately £2,500, then the Rolls-Royce Merlin engine at £2,000, followed by the wings at £1,800 a pair, guns and undercarriage, both at £800 each, and the propeller at £350.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_2006.2C_p._61_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_2006.2C_p._61-35">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing_at_Castle_Bromwich.2C_Birmingham">Manufacturing at Castle Bromwich, Birmingham</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Manufacturing at Castle Bromwich, Birmingham">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_IIA_P7666.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="429" data-file-width="899" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Spitfire_IIA_P7666.jpg/220px-Spitfire_IIA_P7666.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Spitfire_IIA_P7666.jpg/330px-Spitfire_IIA_P7666.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Spitfire_IIA_P7666.jpg/440px-Spitfire_IIA_P7666.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_IIA_P7666.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spitfire Mk IIA, P7666, EB-Z, "Observer Corps", was built at Castle Bromwich, and delivered to <a href="/wiki/No._41_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 41 Squadron RAF">41 Squadron</a> on 23 November 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[nb 5]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bromwich_Assembly" title="Castle Bromwich Assembly">Castle Bromwich Assembly</a></div>
<p>In 1935, the Air Ministry approached <a href="/wiki/Morris_Motors" title="Morris Motors">Morris Motors Limited</a> to ask how quickly their <a href="/wiki/Plant_Oxford" title="Plant Oxford">Cowley plant</a> could be turned to aircraft production. In 1936 this informal request for major manufacturing facilities was turned into a formal scheme to boost British aircraft production capacity under the leadership of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbert_Austin" title="Herbert Austin">Herbert Austin</a>, known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shadow_factory_plan" title="Shadow factory plan">Shadow factory plan</a>. Austin was given the task of building nine new factories, and to supplement the existing British car manufacturing industry by either adding to overall capacity or increasing the potential for reorganisation to produce aircraft and their engines.</p>
<p>Under the plan, on 12 July 1938, the Air Ministry bought a site consisting of farm fields and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sewage_works" title="Sewage works">sewage works</a> next to <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bromwich_Aerodrome" title="Castle Bromwich Aerodrome">Castle Bromwich Aerodrome</a> in Birmingham. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shadow_factory" title="Shadow factory">shadow factory</a> would supplement Supermarine's original factories in Southampton in building the Spitfire. The <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bromwich_Assembly" title="Castle Bromwich Assembly">Castle Bromwich Aircraft Factory</a> ordered the most modern <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">machine tools</a> then available, which were being installed two months after work started on the site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._67_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._67-4">[4]</a></sup> Although Morris Motors under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Nuffield" title="Lord Nuffield">Lord Nuffield</a> (an expert in mass motor-vehicle construction) at first managed and equipped the factory, it was funded by government money. When the project was first mooted it was estimated that the factory would be built for £2,000,000, however, by the beginning of 1939 this cost had doubled to over £4,000,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._145_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._145-37">[32]</a></sup> The Spitfire's stressed-skin construction required precision engineering skills and techniques outside the experience of the local labour force, which took some time to train. However, even as the first Spitfires were being built in June 1940 the factory was still incomplete, and there were numerous problems with the factory management, which ignored tooling and drawings provided by Supermarine in favour of tools and drawings of its own designs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[33]</a></sup> and with the workforce which, while not completely stopping production, continually threatened strikes or "slow downs" until their demands for higher than average pay rates were met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>By May 1940, Castle Bromwich had not yet built its first Spitfire, in spite of promises that the factory would be producing 60 per week starting in April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._145_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._145-37">[32]</a></sup> On 17 May <a href="/wiki/Max_Aitken,_1st_Baron_Beaverbrook" title="Max Aitken, 1st Baron Beaverbrook">Lord Beaverbrook</a>, Minister of Aircraft Production, telephoned Lord Nuffield and manoeuvred him into handing over control of the Castle Bromwich plant to Beaverbook's Ministry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup> Beaverbrook immediately sent in experienced management staff and experienced workers from Supermarine and gave over control of the factory to Vickers-Armstrong. Although it would take some time to resolve the problems, in June 1940, 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_variants" title="Supermarine Spitfire variants">Mk IIs</a> were built; 23 rolled out in July, 37 in August, and 56 in September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup> By the time production ended at Castle Bromwich in June 1945, a total of 12,129 Spitfires (921 Mk IIs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Air_International_1985_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Air_International_1985-42">[37]</a></sup> 4,489 Mk Vs, 5,665 Mk IXs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup> and 1,054 Mk XVIs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Air_International_1985_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Air_International_1985-42">[37]</a></sup>) had been built. CBAF went on to become the largest and most successful plant of its type during the 1939–45 conflict. As the largest Spitfire factory in the UK, by producing a maximum of 320 aircraft per month, it built over half of the approximately 20,000 aircraft of this type.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_dispersal">Production dispersal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Production dispersal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_mk11_pl965_arp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1484" data-file-width="2192" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Spitfire_mk11_pl965_arp.jpg/220px-Spitfire_mk11_pl965_arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Spitfire_mk11_pl965_arp.jpg/330px-Spitfire_mk11_pl965_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Spitfire_mk11_pl965_arp.jpg/440px-Spitfire_mk11_pl965_arp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_mk11_pl965_arp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This Spitfire PR Mk XI (PL965) was built at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Aldermaston" title="RAF Aldermaston">RAF Aldermaston</a> in southern England</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the Battle of Britain, concerted efforts were made by the <i>Luftwaffe</i> to destroy the main manufacturing plants at Woolston and <a href="/wiki/River_Itchen,_Hampshire" title="River Itchen, Hampshire">Itchen</a>, near Southampton. The first bombing raid, which missed the factories, came on 23 August 1940. Over the next month, other raids were mounted until, on 26 September 1940, both factories were completely wrecked,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._115_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._115-44">[39]</a></sup> with 92 people being killed and a large number injured; most of the casualties were experienced aircraft production workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smallwood_1996_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smallwood_1996-45">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fortunately for the future of the Spitfire, many of the production jigs and machine tools had already been relocated by 20 September, and steps were being taken to disperse production to small facilities throughout the Southampton area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._115_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._115-44">[39]</a></sup> To this end, the British government requisitioned the likes of Vincent's Garage in Station Square <a href="/wiki/Reading,_Berkshire" title="Reading, Berkshire">Reading</a>, which later specialised in manufacturing Spitfire fuselages, and Anna Valley Motors, <a href="/wiki/Salisbury" title="Salisbury">Salisbury</a>, which was to become the sole producer of the wing leading-edge fuel tanks for photo-reconnaissance Spitfires, as well as producing other components. A purpose-built works, specialising in manufacturing fuselages and installing engines, was built at Star Road, <a href="/wiki/Caversham,_Berkshire" title="Caversham, Berkshire">Caversham</a> in Reading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smallwood_1996_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smallwood_1996-45">[40]</a></sup> The drawing office in which all Spitfire designs were drafted was relocated to <a href="/wiki/Hursley_House" title="Hursley House">Hursley Park</a>, near Southampton. This site also had an aircraft assembly hangar where many prototype and experimental Spitfires were assembled, but since it had no associated aerodrome no Spitfires ever flew from Hursley.</p>
<p>Four towns and their satellite airfields were chosen to be the focal points for these workshops:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._115_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._115-44">[39]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Southampton and <a href="/wiki/Eastleigh" title="Eastleigh">Eastleigh</a> Airport</li>
<li>Salisbury with High Post and Chattis Hill aerodromes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[nb 6]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Trowbridge" title="Trowbridge">Trowbridge</a> with <a href="/wiki/RAF_Keevil" title="RAF Keevil">Keevil</a> aerodrome</li>
<li>Reading with <a href="/wiki/Henley-on-Thames" title="Henley-on-Thames">Henley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aldermaston" title="Aldermaston">Aldermaston</a> aerodromes.</li>
<li>An experimental factory at Newbury was the subject of a <i>Luftwaffe</i> daylight raid but all missed their target and bombed a nearby school.</li>
</ul>
<p>Completed Spitfires were delivered to the airfields on large <a href="/wiki/Commer" title="Commer">Commer</a> "<a href="/wiki/Queen_Mary_trailer" title="Queen Mary trailer">Queen Mary</a>" low-loader articulated trucks, there to be fully assembled, tested, then passed on to the RAF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smallwood_1996_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Smallwood_1996-45">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_testing">Flight testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Flight testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>All production Spitfires were flight tested before delivery. During the Second World War, <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Quill" title="Jeffrey Quill">Jeffrey Quill</a> was Vickers Supermarine's chief test pilot, in charge of flight-testing all aircraft types built by Vickers Supermarine; he also oversaw a group of 10 to 12 pilots responsible for testing all developmental and production Spitfires built by the company in the Southampton area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[nb 7]</a></sup> Quill had also devised the standard testing procedures which, with variations for specific aircraft designs, operated from 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Test_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Test-49">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alex_Henshaw" title="Alex Henshaw">Alex Henshaw</a>, chief test pilot at Castle Bromwich from 1940, was placed in charge of testing all Spitfires built at that factory, co-ordinating a team of 25 pilots; he also assessed all Spitfire developments. Between 1940 and 1946, Henshaw flew a total of 2,360 Spitfires and Seafires, more than 10% of total production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Henshaw wrote about flight testing Spitfires:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>After a thorough pre-flight check I would take off and, once at circuit height, I would trim the aircraft and try to get her to fly straight and level with hands off the stick ... Once the trim was satisfactory I would take the Spitfire up in a full-throttle climb at 2,850 rpm to the rated altitude of one or both supercharger blowers. Then I would make a careful check of the power output from the engine, calibrated for height and temperature ... If all appeared satisfactory I would then put her into a dive at full power and 3,000 rpm, and trim her to fly hands and feet off at 460 mph (740 km/h) IAS (Indicated Air Speed). Personally, I never cleared a Spitfire unless I had carried out a few aerobatic tests to determine how good or bad she was.</p>
<p>The production test was usually quite a brisk affair: the initial circuit lasted less than ten minutes and the main flight took between twenty and thirty minutes. Then the aircraft received a final once-over by our ground mechanics, any faults were rectified and the Spitfire was ready for collection.</p>
I loved the Spitfire in all of her many versions. But I have to admit that the later marks, although they were faster than the earlier ones, were also much heavier and so did not handle so well. You did not have such positive control over them. One test of manoeuvrability was to throw her into a flick-roll and see how many times she rolled. With the Mark II or the Mark V one got two-and-a-half flick-rolls but the Mark IX was heavier and you got only one-and-a-half. With the later and still heavier versions, one got even less. The essence of aircraft design is compromise, and an improvement at one end of the performance envelope is rarely achieved without a deterioration somewhere else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[46]</a></sup></blockquote>
<p>When the last Spitfire rolled out in February 1948,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[47]</a></sup> a total of 20,351 examples of all variants had been built, including two-seat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trainer_aircraft" title="Trainer aircraft">trainers</a>, with some Spitfires remaining in service well into the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethell_p._117_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ethell_p._117-3">[3]</a></sup> The Spitfire was the only British fighter aircraft to be in continuous production before, during and after the Second World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._6_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._6-55">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Airframe">Airframe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Airframe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_mk2a_p7350_arp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1370" data-file-width="1305" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Spitfire_mk2a_p7350_arp.jpg/220px-Spitfire_mk2a_p7350_arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Spitfire_mk2a_p7350_arp.jpg/330px-Spitfire_mk2a_p7350_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Spitfire_mk2a_p7350_arp.jpg/440px-Spitfire_mk2a_p7350_arp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_mk2a_p7350_arp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spitfire Mk IIa <i>P7350</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Memorial_Flight" title="Battle of Britain Memorial Flight">BBMF</a> is the only existing airworthy Spitfire that fought in the Battle of Britain.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the mid-1930s, aviation design teams worldwide started developing a new generation of all-metal, low-wing fighter aircraft. The French <a href="/wiki/Dewoitine_D.520" title="Dewoitine D.520">Dewoitine D.520</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[49]</a></sup> and Germany's <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Bf_109" title="Messerschmitt Bf 109">Messerschmitt Bf 109</a>, for example, were designed to take advantage of new techniques of <a href="/wiki/Monocoque" title="Monocoque">monocoque</a> construction and the availability of new high-powered, liquid-cooled, in-line aero engines. They also featured refinements such as retractable undercarriages, fully enclosed cockpits and low drag, all-metal wings (all introduced on civil airliners years before but slow to be adopted by the military, who favoured the biplane's simplicity and manoeuvrability).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mitchell's design aims were to create a well-balanced, high-performance bomber interceptor and fighter aircraft capable of fully exploiting the power of the Merlin engine, while being relatively easy to fly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_2002.2C_p._20_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_2002.2C_p._20-58">[51]</a></sup> At the time, with <a href="/wiki/Entente_Cordiale" title="Entente Cordiale">France as an ally</a>, and Germany thought to be the most likely future opponent, no enemy fighters were expected to appear over Great Britain, enemy bombers would be required to fly to the UK over the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> and no single engined fighter at the time had the range to accompany the bombers and successfully return to its own friendly airfields; to carry out the mission of home defence, the design was intended to climb quickly to meet enemy bombers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodie20_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodie20-59">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Spitfire's airframe was complex: the streamlined, <a href="/wiki/Semi-monocoque" title="Semi-monocoque">semi-monocoque</a> <a href="/wiki/Duralumin" title="Duralumin">duralumin</a> fuselage featured a large number of compound curves built up from a skeleton of 19 <a href="/wiki/Former" title="Former">formers, also known as frames</a>, starting from frame number one, immediately behind the propeller unit, to the tail unit attachment frame. The first four frames supported the glycol header tank and engine cowlings. Frame 5, to which the engine bearers were secured, supported the weight of the engine and accessories, and the loads imposed by the engine: this was a strengthened double frame which also incorporated the fireproof bulkhead and, in later versions of the Spitfire, the oil tank. This frame also tied the four main fuselage <a href="/wiki/Longeron" title="Longeron">longerons</a> to the rest of the airframe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[53]</a></sup> Behind the bulkhead were five 'U' shaped half-frames which accommodated the fuel tanks and cockpit. The rear fuselage started at the eleventh frame, to which the pilot's seat and (later) armour plating was attached, and ended at the nineteenth, which was mounted at a slight forward angle just forward of the fin. Each of these nine frames were oval, reducing in size towards the tail, and incorporated several <a href="/wiki/Lightening_holes" title="Lightening holes">lightening holes</a> to reduce their weight as much as possible without weakening them. The U-shaped frame 20 was the last frame of the fuselage proper and the frame to which the tail unit was attached. Frames 21, 22 and 23 formed the fin; frame 22 incorporated the tailwheel opening and frame 23 was the <a href="/wiki/Flight_control_surfaces" title="Flight control surfaces">rudder</a> post. Before being attached to the main fuselage, the tail unit frames were held in a jig and the eight horizontal tail formers were riveted to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p><br/>
A combination of 14 <a href="/wiki/Longeron" title="Longeron">longitudinal stringers</a> and four main longerons attached to the frames helped form a light but rigid structure to which sheets of <a href="/wiki/Alclad" title="Alclad">alclad</a> stressed skinning were attached. The fuselage plating was 24, 20 and 18 <a href="/wiki/Sheet_metal" title="Sheet metal">gauge</a> in order of thickness towards the tail, while the fin structure was completed using short longerons from frames 20 to 23, before being covered in 22 gauge plating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The skins of the fuselage, wings and tailplane were secured by rivets and in critical areas such as the wing forward of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spar_(aviation)" title="Spar (aviation)">main spar</a> where an uninterrupted airflow was required, with flush rivets; the fuselage used standard dome-headed riveting. From February 1943 flush riveting was used on the fuselage, affecting all Spitfire variants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[56]</a></sup> In some areas, such as at the rear of the wing, and the lower tailplane skins the top was riveted and the bottom fixed by brass screws which tapped into strips of <a href="/wiki/Spruce" title="Spruce">spruce</a> bolted to the lower ribs. The removable wing tips were made up of duralumin skinned spruce formers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[57]</a></sup> At first the ailerons, elevators and rudder were fabric-covered. When combat experience showed that fabric-covered ailerons were impossible to use at high speeds, a light alloy replaced the fabric, enhancing control throughout the speed range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elliptical_wing_design">Elliptical wing design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Elliptical wing design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1934, Mitchell and the design staff decided to use a semi-elliptical wing shape to solve two conflicting requirements; the wing needed to be thin, to avoid creating too much <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a>, while still able to house a retractable undercarriage, plus armament and ammunition. An elliptical planform is the most efficient aerodynamic shape for an untwisted wing, leading to the lowest amount of <a href="/wiki/Lift-induced_drag" title="Lift-induced drag">induced drag</a>. The ellipse was skewed so that the centre of pressure, which occurs at the quarter-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chord_(aircraft)" title="Chord (aircraft)">chord</a> position, aligned with the main spar, thus preventing the wings from twisting. Mitchell has sometimes been accused of copying the wing shape of the <a href="/wiki/Heinkel_He_70" title="Heinkel He 70">Heinkel He 70</a>, which first flew in 1932; but as <a href="/wiki/Beverley_Shenstone" title="Beverley Shenstone">Beverley Shenstone</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aerodynamics" title="Aerodynamics">aerodynamicist</a> on Mitchell's team, explained "Our wing was much thinner and had quite a different section to that of the Heinkel. In any case it would have been simply asking for trouble to have copied a wing shape from an aircraft designed for an entirely different purpose."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-document_pp._33.E2.80.9334_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-document_pp._33.E2.80.9334-66">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[nb 8]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:25%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>The elliptical wing was decided upon quite early on. Aerodynamically it was the best for our purpose because the <a href="/wiki/Lift-induced_drag" title="Lift-induced drag">induced drag</a> caused in producing lift, was lowest when this shape was used: the ellipse was ... theoretically a perfection ... To reduce drag we wanted the lowest possible thickness-to-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chord_(aircraft)" title="Chord (aircraft)">chord</a>, consistent with the necessary strength. But near the root the wing had to be thick enough to accommodate the retracted undercarriages and the guns ... Mitchell was an intensely practical man ... The ellipse was simply the shape that allowed us the thinnest possible wing with room inside to carry the necessary structure and the things we wanted to cram in. And it looked nice.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><i>Beverly Shenstone</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[61]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The wing section used was from the <a href="/wiki/NACA_airfoil" title="NACA airfoil">NACA 2200 series</a>, which had been adapted to create a thickness-to-chord ratio of 13% at the root, reducing to 9.4% at the tip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[62]</a></sup> A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dihedral_(aircraft)" title="Dihedral (aircraft)">dihedral</a> of six degrees was adopted to give increased lateral stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_2002.2C_p._20_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price_2002.2C_p._20-58">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>A feature of the wing which contributed greatly to its success was an innovative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spar_(aviation)" title="Spar (aviation)">spar</a> boom design, made up of five square tubes that fitted into each other. As the wing thinned out along its span the tubes were progressively cut away in a similar fashion to a <a href="/wiki/Leaf_spring" title="Leaf spring">leaf spring</a>; two of these booms were linked together by an alloy web, creating a lightweight and very strong main spar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_2002.2C_p._19._71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_2002.2C_p._19.-71">[63]</a></sup> The undercarriage legs were attached to pivot points built into the inner, rear section of the main spar and retracted outwards and slightly backwards into wells in the non-load-carrying wing structure. The resultant narrow undercarriage track was considered to be an acceptable compromise as this reduced the bending loads on the main-spar during landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_2002.2C_p._19._71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price_2002.2C_p._19.-71">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ahead of the spar, the thick-skinned leading edge of the wing formed a strong and rigid D-shaped box, which took most of the wing loads. At the time the wing was designed, this D-shaped leading edge was intended to house steam condensers for the evaporative cooling system intended for the PV-XII. Constant problems with the evaporative system in the <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Goshawk" title="Rolls-Royce Goshawk">Goshawk</a> led to the adoption of a cooling system which used 100% <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">glycol</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[nb 9]</a></sup>. The radiators were housed in a new radiator-duct designed by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fredrick_Meredith&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fredrick Meredith (page does not exist)">Fredrick Meredith</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Aircraft_Establishment" title="Royal Aircraft Establishment">RAE</a> at <a href="/wiki/Farnborough,_Hampshire" title="Farnborough, Hampshire">Farnborough</a>; this used the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meredith_Effect" title="Meredith Effect">cooling air to generate thrust</a>, greatly reducing the net drag produced by the radiators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-document_p._24_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-document_p._24-73">[64]</a></sup> In turn, the leading-edge structure lost its function as a condenser, but it was later adapted to house integral fuel tanks of various sizes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[65]</a></sup> a feature patented by Vickers-Supermarine in 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[66]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_mark19_ps853_planform_arp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1364" data-file-width="1282" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spitfire_mark19_ps853_planform_arp.jpg/220px-Spitfire_mark19_ps853_planform_arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spitfire_mark19_ps853_planform_arp.jpg/330px-Spitfire_mark19_ps853_planform_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Spitfire_mark19_ps853_planform_arp.jpg/440px-Spitfire_mark19_ps853_planform_arp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_mark19_ps853_planform_arp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The elliptical planform of a Spitfire PR.Mk.XIX displayed at an air show in 2008. The black and white <a href="/wiki/Invasion_stripes" title="Invasion stripes">Invasion stripes</a> are visible.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another feature of the wing was its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washout_(aviation)" title="Washout (aviation)">washout</a>. The trailing edge of the wing twisted slightly upward along its span, the <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_incidence_(aerodynamics)" title="Angle of incidence (aerodynamics)">angle of incidence</a> decreasing from +2° at its root to -½° at its tip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews_super.2C_p._216_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews_super.2C_p._216-76">[67]</a></sup> This caused the wing roots to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stall_(aerodynamic)" title="Stall (aerodynamic)">stall</a> before the tips, reducing tip-stall that could otherwise have resulted in a wing drop, often leading to a spin. As the wing roots started to stall, the separating air stream started to buffet (vibrate) the aircraft, warning the pilot, and hence allowing even relatively inexperienced pilots to fly the aircraft to the limits of its performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[68]</a></sup> This washout was first featured in the wing of the Type 224 and became a consistent feature in subsequent designs leading to the Spitfire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[69]</a></sup> The complexity of the wing design, especially the precision required to manufacture the vital spar and leading-edge structures, at first caused some major hold-ups in the production of the Spitfire. The problems increased when the work was put out to subcontractors, most of whom had never dealt with metal-structured, high-speed aircraft. By June 1939, most of these problems had been resolved, and production was no longer held up by a lack of wings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>All of the main flight controls were originally metal structures with fabric covering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[nb 10]</a></sup>Designers and pilots felt that having ailerons which were too heavy to move (in terms of effort, not mass) at high speed would avoid possible aileron reversal, stopping pilots throwing the aircraft around and pulling the wings off. It was also felt that air combat would take place at relatively low speed and that high-speed manoeuvring would be physically impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[72]</a></sup> During the Battle of Britain, pilots found the ailerons of the Spitfire were far too heavy at high speeds, severely restricting lateral manoeuvres such as rolls and high-speed turns, which were still a feature of air-to-air combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[73]</a></sup> Flight tests showed the fabric covering of the ailerons "ballooned" at high speeds, adversely affecting the aerodynamics. Replacing the fabric covering with light alloy dramatically improved the ailerons at high speed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[74]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_VII_Langley_USA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="553" data-file-width="720" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Spitfire_VII_Langley_USA.jpg/220px-Spitfire_VII_Langley_USA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Spitfire_VII_Langley_USA.jpg/330px-Spitfire_VII_Langley_USA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Spitfire_VII_Langley_USA.jpg/440px-Spitfire_VII_Langley_USA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_VII_Langley_USA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spitfire HF Mk VII. The shape of the ellipse was altered by the extended "pointed" wing tips used by the high-altitude Mk VI and VIIs, and some early Mk VIIIs.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Spitfire had detachable wing tips which were secured by two mounting points at the end of each main wing assembly: when the Spitfire took on a role as a high-altitude fighter (Marks VI and VII and some early Mk VIIIs) the standard wing tips were replaced by extended, "pointed" tips which increased the wingspan from 36 ft 10 in (11.23 m) to 40 ft 2 in (12.3 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[75]</a></sup> The other wing tip variation, used by several Spitfire variants, was the "clipped" wing; the standard wing tips were replaced by wooden fairings which reduced the span to 32 ft 6 in (9.9 m)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[76]</a></sup> The wing tips used <a href="/wiki/Spruce" title="Spruce">spruce</a> formers for most of the internal structure with a light alloy skin attached using brass screws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The airflow through the main radiator was controlled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pneumatic" title="Pneumatic">pneumatic</a> exit flaps. In early marks of Spitfire (Mk I to Mk VI) the single flap was operated manually using a lever to the left of the pilot's seat. When the two-stage Merlin was introduced in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_(late_Merlin_powered_variants)" title="Supermarine Spitfire (late Merlin powered variants)">Spitfire Mk IX</a> the radiators were split to make room for an intercooler radiator; the radiator under the starboard wing was halved in size and the intercooler radiator housed alongside. Under the port wing a new radiator fairing housed a square oil cooler alongside of the other half-radiator unit. The two radiator flaps were now operated automatically via a <a href="/wiki/Thermostat" title="Thermostat">thermostat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The light alloy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flap_(aircraft)" title="Flap (aircraft)">split flaps</a> at the trailing edge of the wing were also pneumatically operated via a finger lever on the instrument panel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[79]</a></sup> Only two positions were available; fully up or fully down (85°). The flaps were normally lowered only during the final approach and for landing, and the pilot was to retract them before taxiing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[nb 11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ellipse also served as the design basis for the Spitfire's fin and tailplane assembly, once again exploiting the shape's favourable aerodynamic characteristics. Both the elevators and rudder were shaped so that their centre of mass was shifted forward, thus reducing control-surface flutter. The longer noses and greater propeller-wash resulting from larger engines in later models necessitated increasingly larger vertical and, later, horizontal tail surfaces to compensate for the altered aerodynamics, culminating in those of the Mk 22/24 series which were 25% larger in area than those of the Mk I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Improved_late_wing_designs">Improved late wing designs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Improved late wing designs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As the Spitfire gained more power and was able to manoeuvre at higher speeds, the possibility that pilots would encounter <a href="/wiki/Control_reversal" title="Control reversal">aileron reversal</a> increased, and the Supermarine design team set about redesigning the wings to counter this. The original wing design had a <i>theoretical</i> aileron reversal speed of 580 mph (930 km/h),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quill_1983.2C_p._272._94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quill_1983.2C_p._272.-94">[83]</a></sup> which was somewhat lower than that of some contemporary fighters. The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Aircraft_Establishment" title="Royal Aircraft Establishment">Royal Aircraft Establishment</a> noted that, at 400 mph (640 km/h) <a href="/wiki/Indicated_airspeed" title="Indicated airspeed">IAS</a>, roughly 65% of aileron effectiveness was lost, due to wing twist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>The new wing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_(late_Merlin_powered_variants)" title="Supermarine Spitfire (late Merlin powered variants)">Spitfire F Mk 21</a> and its successors was designed to help alleviate this problem; the wing's stiffness was increased by 47%, and a new design of aileron using <a href="/wiki/Hinge" title="Hinge">piano hinges</a> and geared <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trim_tabs" title="Trim tabs">trim tabs</a> meant that the theoretical aileron reversal speed was increased to 825 mph (1,328 km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quill_1983.2C_p._272._94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quill_1983.2C_p._272.-94">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[86]</a></sup> Alongside the redesigned wing Supermarine also experimented with the original wing, raising the leading edge by one inch (2.54 cm), with the hope of improving pilot view and reducing drag. This wing was tested on a modified F Mk 21, also called the F Mk 23, (sometimes referred to as "Valiant" rather than "Spitfire"). The increase in performance was minimal and this experiment was abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_WoF_pt2.2C_p._57_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_WoF_pt2.2C_p._57-98">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Supermarine developed a new <a href="/wiki/Laminar_flow" title="Laminar flow">laminar flow</a> wing based on new aerofoil profiles developed by NACA in the United States, with the objective of reducing drag and improving performance. These laminar flow airfoils were the Supermarine 371-I used at the root and the 371-II used at the tip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[88]</a></sup> Supermarine estimated that the new wing could give an increase in speed of 55 mph (89 km/h) over the Spitfire Mk 21.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews.26_Morgan.2C_p._264_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews.26_Morgan.2C_p._264-100">[89]</a></sup> The new wing was initially fitted to a Spitfire Mk XIV; later a new fuselage was designed, with the new fighter becoming the <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spiteful" title="Supermarine Spiteful">Supermarine Spiteful</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews.26Morgan_pp._264.E2.80.936_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews.26Morgan_pp._264.E2.80.936-101">[90]</a></sup> Somewhat surprisingly, the new trapezoid wing had lower critical Mach number than the old elliptic one, bringing one of the test pilots who flew the "jet Spiteful" (the <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Attacker" title="Supermarine Attacker">Supermarine Attacker</a>) to remark that the original wing should have been retained on the jet.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carburetion_versus_fuel_injection">Carburetion versus fuel injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Carburetion versus fuel injection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early in its development, the Merlin engine's lack of <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">fuel injection</a> meant that Spitfires and Hurricanes, unlike the Bf 109E, were unable to simply nose down into a steep dive. This meant a <i>Luftwaffe</i> fighter could simply "bunt" into a high-power dive to escape an attack, leaving the Spitfire behind, as its fuel was forced out of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carburettor" title="Carburettor">carburettor</a> by <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">negative "g"</a>. RAF fighter pilots soon learned to "half-roll" their aircraft before diving to pursue their opponents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[91]</a></sup> Carburettors were adopted because, as Sir <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Hooker" title="Stanley Hooker">Stanley Hooker</a> explained, the carburettor "increased the performance of the supercharger and thereby increased the power of the engine."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._206._103-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._206.-103">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[nb 12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1941, a metal disc with a hole was fitted in the fuel line, restricting fuel flow to the maximum the engine could consume. While it did not cure the problem of the initial <a href="/wiki/Fuel_starvation" title="Fuel starvation">fuel starvation</a> in a dive, it did reduce the more serious problem of the carburettor being flooded with fuel by the fuel pumps under negative "g". It became known as "<a href="/wiki/Miss_Shilling%27s_orifice" title="Miss Shilling's orifice">Miss Shilling's orifice</a>" as it was invented by <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Shilling" title="Beatrice Shilling">Beatrice "Tilly" Shilling</a>. Further improvements were introduced throughout the Merlin series, with <a href="/wiki/Bendix_Corporation" title="Bendix Corporation">Bendix</a>-manufactured <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_carburettor" title="Pressure carburettor">pressure carburettors</a>, which were designed to allow fuel to flow during all flight attitudes, introduced in 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._206._103-1"><a href="#cite_note-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._206.-103">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armament">Armament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Armament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to a shortage of Brownings, which had been selected as the new standard rifle calibre machine gun for the RAF in 1934, early Spitfires were fitted with only four guns, with the other four fitted later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_p._93_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_p._93-106">[94]</a></sup> Early tests showed that while the guns worked perfectly on the ground and at low altitudes, they tended to freeze at high altitude, especially the outer wing guns. This was because the RAF's Brownings had been modified to fire from an open bolt; while this prevented <a href="/wiki/Cooking_off" title="Cooking off">overheating</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cordite" title="Cordite">cordite</a> used in British ammunition, it allowed cold air to flow through the barrel unhindered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_pp._16.2C_93-94_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_pp._16.2C_93-94-107">[95]</a></sup> Supermarine did not fix the problem until October 1938, when they added hot air ducts from the rear of the wing mounted radiators to the guns, and bulkheads around the gunbays to trap the hot air in the wing. Red fabric patches were <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_dope" title="Aircraft dope">doped</a> over the gun ports to protect the guns from cold, dirt and moisture until they were fired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_pp._93.E2.80.934_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_pp._93.E2.80.934-108">[96]</a></sup> Even if the eight Brownings worked perfectly, pilots soon discovered that they were not sufficient to destroy larger aircraft. Combat reports showed that an average of 4,500 rounds were needed to shoot down an enemy aircraft. In November 1938, tests against armoured and unarmoured targets had already indicated that the introduction of a weapon of at least 20 mm calibre was urgently needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_p._95_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_p._95-109">[97]</a></sup> A variant on the Spitfire design with four 20 mm Oerlikon cannon had been tendered to specification F37/35 but the order for prototypes had gone to the <a href="/wiki/Westland_Whirlwind_(fighter)" title="Westland Whirlwind (fighter)">Westland Whirlwind</a> in January 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 1939, a Spitfire was fitted with a drum-fed <a href="/wiki/Hispano-Suiza_HS.404#British_production" title="Hispano-Suiza HS.404">Hispano</a> in each wing, an installation that required large blisters on the wing to cover the 60-round drum. The cannon suffered frequent stoppages, mostly because the guns were mounted on their sides to fit as much of the magazine as possible within the wing. In January 1940, P/O George Proudman flew this prototype in combat, but the starboard gun stopped after firing a single round, while the port gun fired 30 rounds before seizing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_pp._93.E2.80.934_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_pp._93.E2.80.934-108">[96]</a></sup> If one cannon seized, the recoil of the other threw the aircraft off aim. Nevertheless, 30 more cannon-armed Spitfires were ordered for operational trials, and they were soon known as the Mk IB, to distinguish them from the Browning-armed Mk IA, and were delivered to No. 19 Squadron beginning in June 1940. The Hispanos were found to be so unreliable that the squadron requested an exchange of its aircraft with the older Browning-armed aircraft of an operational training unit. By August, Supermarine had perfected a more reliable installation with an improved feed mechanism and four .303s in the outer wing panels. The modified fighters were then delivered to 19 Squadron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_pp._93.E2.80.934_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_pp._93.E2.80.934-108">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operational_history">Operational history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Operational history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Service_operations">Service operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Service operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_operational_history" title="Supermarine Spitfire operational history">Supermarine Spitfire operational history</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpitI19a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="800" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/SpitI19a.jpg/220px-SpitI19a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/SpitI19a.jpg/330px-SpitI19a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/SpitI19a.jpg/440px-SpitI19a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpitI19a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>K9795</i>, the 9th production Mk I, with <a href="/wiki/No._19_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 19 Squadron RAF">19 Squadron</a> in 1938.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The operational history of the Spitfire with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAF" title="RAF">RAF</a> started with the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_variants" title="Supermarine Spitfire variants">Mk Is</a> <i>K9789</i>, which entered service with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/No._XIX_Squadron_RAF" title="No. XIX Squadron RAF">19 Squadron</a> at <a href="/wiki/Duxford_Aerodrome" title="Duxford Aerodrome">RAF Duxford</a> on 4 August 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._67_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._67-4">[4]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[nb 13]</a></sup> The Spitfire achieved legendary status during the Battle of Britain, a reputation aided by the famous "Spitfire Fund" organised and run by <a href="/wiki/Max_Aitken,_1st_Baron_Beaverbrook" title="Max Aitken, 1st Baron Beaverbrook">Lord Beaverbrook</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Aircraft_Production" title="Minister of Aircraft Production">Minister of Aircraft Production</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[99]</a></sup> In fact the Hurricane outnumbered the Spitfire throughout the battle, and shouldered the burden of the defence against the Luftwaffe; however, because of its higher performance the overall attrition rate of the Spitfire squadrons was lower than that of the Hurricane units, and the Spitfire units had a higher victory-to-loss ratio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[100]</a></sup> The key aim of Fighter Command was to stop the <i>Luftwaffe's</i> bombers; in practice the tactic, whenever possible, was to use Spitfires to counter German escort fighters, by then based in northern France, particularly the Bf 109s, while the Hurricane squadrons attacked the bombers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[101]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_VC_103_MU.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="410" data-file-width="1465" height="62" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Spitfire_VC_103_MU.jpg/220px-Spitfire_VC_103_MU.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Spitfire_VC_103_MU.jpg/330px-Spitfire_VC_103_MU.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Spitfire_VC_103_MU.jpg/440px-Spitfire_VC_103_MU.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_VC_103_MU.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Supermarine Spitfire Mk.VC, BR114, of the No 103 MU, Aboukir, 1942</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Well-known Spitfire pilots included <a href="/wiki/Johnnie_Johnson_(RAF_officer)" title="Johnnie Johnson (RAF officer)">"Johnnie" Johnson</a> (34 enemy aircraft shot down),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[102]</a></sup> who flew the Spitfire right through his operational career from late 1940 to 1945. <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Bader" title="Douglas Bader">Douglas Bader</a> (20 e/a) and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stanford_Tuck" title="Robert Stanford Tuck">"Bob" Tuck</a> (27 e/a) flew Spitfires and Hurricanes during the major air battles of 1940, and both were shot down and became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners-of-war" title="Prisoners-of-war">prisoners of war</a> while flying Spitfires over France in 1941 and 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[103]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paddy_Finucane" title="Paddy Finucane">Paddy Finucane</a> (28–32 e/a) scored all his successes in the fighter before disappearing over the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> in July 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[104]</a></sup> Some notable Commonwealth pilots were <a href="/wiki/George_Beurling" title="George Beurling">George Beurling</a> (31<span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>3</sub></span> e/a) from Canada, <a href="/wiki/Adolph_Malan" title="Adolph Malan">"Sailor" Malan</a> (27 e/a) from South Africa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[105]</a></sup> New Zealanders <a href="/wiki/Alan_Christopher_Deere" title="Alan Christopher Deere">Alan Deere</a> (17 e/a) and <a href="/wiki/Colin_Falkland_Gray" title="Colin Falkland Gray">C F Gray</a> (27 e/a)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[107]</a></sup> and the Australian Hugo Armstrong (12 e/a).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Spitfire continued to play increasingly diverse roles throughout the Second World War and beyond, often in air forces other than the RAF. The Spitfire, for example, became the first high-speed <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">photo-reconnaissance</a> aircraft to be operated by the RAF. Sometimes unarmed, they flew at high, medium and low altitudes, often ranging far into enemy territory to closely observe the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a> and provide an almost continual flow of valuable intelligence information throughout the war. In 1941 and 1942, PRU Spitfires provided the first photographs of the <i><a href="/wiki/Freya_radar" title="Freya radar">Freya</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrzburg_radar" title="Würzburg radar"><i>Würzburg</i> radar</a> systems and, in 1943, helped confirm that the Germans were building the <a href="/wiki/V-1_flying_bomb" title="V-1 flying bomb">V1</a> and <a href="/wiki/V-2_rocket" title="V-2 rocket">V2</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vergeltungswaffe" title="Vergeltungswaffe">Vergeltungswaffe</a></i> ("vengeance weapons") by photographing <a href="/wiki/Peenem%C3%BCnde" title="Peenemünde">Peenemünde</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> coast of Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Mediterranean the Spitfire blunted the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Malta_(World_War_II)" title="Siege of Malta (World War II)">heavy attacks on Malta</a> by the <i><a href="/wiki/Regia_Aeronautica" title="Regia Aeronautica">Regia Aeronautica</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a></i> and, from early 1943, helped pave the way for the Allied invasions of <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and Italy. On 7 March 1942, 15 Mk Vs carrying 90-gallon fuel tanks under their bellies took off from <a href="/wiki/HMS_Eagle_(1918)" title="HMS Eagle (1918)">HMS <i>Eagle</i></a> off the coast of Algeria on a 600-mile flight to Malta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[110]</a></sup> Those Spitfires V were the first to see service outside Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Spitfire also served on the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Eastern Front</a>: approximately a thousand were supplied to the Soviet Air Force. Though some were used at the frontline in 1943, most of them saw service with the <i><a href="/wiki/Soviet_Air_Defence_Forces" title="Soviet Air Defence Forces">Protivo-Vozdushnaya Oborona</a></i> (English: "Anti-air Defence Branch").</p>
<p>The Spitfire also served in the Pacific Theater. During the Malaya campaign in defense of Singapore, the Spitfire met its match in the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_A6M_Zero" title="Mitsubishi A6M Zero">Mitsubishi A6M Zero</a>. "The RAF pilots were trained in methods that were excellent against German and Italian equipment but suicide against the acrobatic Japs." as <a href="/wiki/Claire_Lee_Chennault" title="Claire Lee Chennault">Lt.Gen. Claire Lee Chennault</a> had to notice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[112]</a></sup> Although not as fast as the Spitfire, the Zero could out-turn the Spitfire with ease, could sustain a climb at a very steep angle, and could stay in the air for three times as long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spick_p.165_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spick_p.165-126">[113]</a></sup> To counter the Zero, Spitfire pilots had to adopt a "slash and run" policy and use their superior speed and diving superiority to fight while avoiding classic dogfights. It also did not help that Southeast Asia was a lower-priority area which was allocated few Spitfires and other modern fighters compared to Europe, which allowed the Japanese to easily achieve air superiority by 1942.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Young_2013_36_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Young_2013_36-127">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[116]</a></sup> Over the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a> of Australia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAAF" title="RAAF">RAAF</a> and RAF Spitfires helped defend the port town of <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">Darwin</a> against air attack by the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Navy_Air_Service" title="Imperial Japanese Navy Air Service">Japanese Naval Air Force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_pp._122.E2.80.933_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_pp._122.E2.80.933-130">[117]</a></sup> Spitfire MKVIII's took part in the last battle of WWII involving the Western allies, in Burma as a ground attack role, helping to defeat a Japanese <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Sittang_Bend" title="Battle of the Sittang Bend">break-out attempt</a>.</p>
<p>During the Second World War, Spitfires were used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USAAF" title="USAAF">USAAF</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4th_Fighter_Group" title="4th Fighter Group">4th Fighter Group</a> until replaced by <a href="/wiki/Republic_P-47_Thunderbolt" title="Republic P-47 Thunderbolt">Republic P-47 Thunderbolts</a> in March 1943.</p>
<p>The Spitfire is listed in the appendix to the novel <i>KG 200</i> as "known to have been regularly flown by" the German secret operations unit <a href="/wiki/Kampfgeschwader_200" title="Kampfgeschwader 200">KG 200</a>, which tested, evaluated and sometimes clandestinely operated captured enemy aircraft during the Second World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[118]</a></sup></p>
