<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speed_and_altitude_records">Speed and altitude records</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Speed and altitude records">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_XI_EN_409.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="709" data-file-width="800" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Spitfire_XI_EN_409.jpg/220px-Spitfire_XI_EN_409.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Spitfire_XI_EN_409.jpg/330px-Spitfire_XI_EN_409.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Spitfire_XI_EN_409.jpg/440px-Spitfire_XI_EN_409.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_XI_EN_409.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Spitfire Mk XI flown by Sqn. Ldr. Martindale, seen here after its flight on 27 April 1944 during which it was damaged achieving a true airspeed of 606 mph (975 km/h or Mach 0.92)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Beginning in late 1943, high-speed diving trials were undertaken at <a href="/wiki/Farnborough,_Hampshire" title="Farnborough, Hampshire">Farnborough</a> to investigate the handling characteristics of aircraft travelling at speeds near the <a href="/wiki/Sound_barrier" title="Sound barrier">sound barrier</a> (i.e., the onset of compressibility effects). Because it had the highest <a href="/wiki/Critical_Mach_number" title="Critical Mach number">limiting Mach number</a> of any aircraft at that time, a Spitfire XI was chosen to take part in these trials. Due to the high altitudes necessary for these dives, a fully feathering <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotol" title="Rotol">Rotol</a> propeller was fitted to prevent <a href="/wiki/Overspeed_(engine)" title="Overspeed (engine)">overspeeding</a>. It was during these trials that <i>EN409</i>, flown by Squadron Leader J. R. Tobin, reached 606 mph (975 km/h, Mach 0.891) in a 45° dive. In April 1944, the same aircraft suffered engine failure in another dive while being flown by Squadron Leader Anthony F. Martindale, <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force_Volunteer_Reserve" title="Royal Air Force Volunteer Reserve">RAFVR</a>, when the propeller and reduction gear broke off. The dive put the aircraft to Mach 0.92, the fastest ever recorded in a piston-engined aircraft, but when the propeller came off the Spitfire, now tail-heavy, zoom-climbed back to altitude. Martindale blacked out under the 11g loading, but when he resumed consciousness he found the aircraft at about 40,000 feet with its (originally straight) wings now slightly swept back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[119]</a></sup> Martindale successfully glided the Spitfire 20 mi (32 km) back to the airfield and landed safely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aircraft_performance_and_design_.28pdf_file.29pp.5.E2.80.936_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aircraft_performance_and_design_.28pdf_file.29pp.5.E2.80.936-133">[120]</a></sup> Martindale was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Cross_(United_Kingdom)" title="Air Force Cross (United Kingdom)">Air Force Cross</a> for his exploits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>A Spitfire was modified by the RAE for high-speed testing of the <a href="/wiki/Stabilator" title="Stabilator">stabilator</a> (then known as the "flying tail") of the <a href="/wiki/Miles_M.52" title="Miles M.52">Miles M.52</a> supersonic research aircraft. RAE test pilot <a href="/wiki/Eric_Brown_(pilot)" title="Eric Brown (pilot)">Eric Brown</a> stated that he tested this successfully during October and November 1944, attaining Mach 0.86 in a dive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Discussion_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Discussion-135">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 5 February 1952, a Spitfire 19 of <a href="/wiki/No._81_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 81 Squadron RAF">81 Squadron</a> based at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Kai_Tak" title="RAF Kai Tak">Kai Tak</a> in Hong Kong reached probably the highest altitude ever achieved by a Spitfire. The pilot, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flight_Lieutenant" title="Flight Lieutenant">Flight Lieutenant</a> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Powles" title="Edward Powles">Edward <i>Ted</i> Cyril Powles</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[123]</a></sup> was on a routine flight to survey outside-air temperature and report on other <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorological</a> conditions at various altitudes in preparation for a proposed new air service through the area. He climbed to 50,000 ft (15,240 m) indicated altitude, with a true altitude of 51,550 ft (15,712 m). The cabin pressure fell below a safe level and, in trying to reduce altitude, he entered an uncontrollable dive which shook the aircraft violently. He eventually regained control somewhere below 3,000 ft (900 m) and landed safely with no discernible damage to his aircraft. Evaluation of the recorded flight data suggested that, in the dive, he achieved a speed of 690 mph (1,110 km/h, Mach 0.96), which would have been the highest speed ever reached by a propeller-driven aircraft, but it has been speculated this figure resulted from inherent instrument errors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aircraft_performance_and_design_.28pdf_file.29pp.5.E2.80.936_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aircraft_performance_and_design_.28pdf_file.29pp.5.E2.80.936-133">[120]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:33%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>That any operational aircraft off the production line, cannons sprouting from its wings and warts and all, could readily be controlled at this speed when the early jet aircraft such as <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Meteor" title="Gloster Meteor">Meteors</a>, <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland_Vampire" title="De Havilland Vampire">Vampires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_P-80_Shooting_Star" title="Lockheed P-80 Shooting Star">P-80s</a>, etc, could not, was certainly extraordinary.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><i>Jeffrey Quill</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[124]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The critical Mach number of the Spitfire's original elliptical wing was higher than the subsequently used laminar-flow-section, straight-tapering-planform wing of the follow-on <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spiteful" title="Supermarine Spiteful">Supermarine Spiteful</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Seafang" title="Supermarine Seafang">Seafang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Attacker" title="Supermarine Attacker">Attacker</a>, illustrating that Reginald Mitchell's practical engineering approach to the problems of high-speed flight had paid off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_(early_Merlin_powered_variants)" title="Supermarine Spitfire (early Merlin powered variants)">Supermarine Spitfire (early Merlin powered variants)</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_(late_Merlin_powered_variants)" title="Supermarine Spitfire (late Merlin powered variants)">Supermarine Spitfire (late Merlin powered variants)</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_(Griffon_powered_variants)" title="Supermarine Spitfire (Griffon powered variants)">Supermarine Spitfire (Griffon powered variants)</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_variants:_specifications,_performance_and_armament" title="Supermarine Spitfire variants: specifications, performance and armament">Supermarine Spitfire variants: specifications, performance and armament</a></div>
<p>Although R. J. Mitchell is justifiably known as the engineer who designed the Spitfire, his premature death in 1937 meant that all development after that date was undertaken by a team led by his chief draughtsman, Joe Smith, who became Supermarine's chief designer on Mitchell's death. As Jeffrey Quill noted: "If Mitchell was born to design the Spitfire, Joe Smith was born to defend and develop it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[126]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpitfireIX611a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="714" data-file-width="1000" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f5/SpitfireIX611a.jpg/220px-SpitfireIX611a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f5/SpitfireIX611a.jpg/330px-SpitfireIX611a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f5/SpitfireIX611a.jpg/440px-SpitfireIX611a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpitfireIX611a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pilots of <a href="/wiki/No._611_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 611 Squadron RAF">611 <i>West Lancashire</i> Squadron</a> pushing an early Spitfire Mark IXb at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAF_Biggin_Hill" title="RAF Biggin Hill">Biggin Hill</a> in late 1942</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There were 24 marks of Spitfire and many sub-variants. These covered the Spitfire in development from the <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">Merlin</a> to <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Griffon" title="Rolls-Royce Griffon">Griffon</a> engines, the high-speed photo-reconnaissance variants and the different wing configurations. More Spitfire Mk Vs were built than any other type, with 6,487 built, followed by the 5,656 Mk IXs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Air_International_1985_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Air_International_1985-42">[37]</a></sup> Different wings, featuring a variety of weapons, were fitted to most marks; the A wing used eight .303 in (7.7 mm) machine guns, the B wing had four .303 in (7.7 mm) machine guns and two 20 mm (.79 in) <a href="/wiki/Hispano-Suiza_HS.404" title="Hispano-Suiza HS.404">Hispano cannon</a>, and the C, or universal, wing could mount either four 20 mm (.79 in) cannon or two 20 mm (.79 in) and four .303 in (7.7 mm) machine guns. As the war progressed, the C wing became more common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flintham_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flintham-140">[127]</a></sup> Another armament variation was the E wing which housed two 20 mm (.79 in) cannon and two .50 in (12.7 mm) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M2_Browning_machine_gun" title="M2 Browning machine gun">Browning machine guns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowyer_p._64_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowyer_p._64-141">[128]</a></sup> Although the Spitfire continued to improve in speed and armament, because of its limited fuel capacity its range and endurance were also limited: it remained "short-legged" throughout its life except in the dedicated photo-reconnaissance role, when its guns were replaced by extra fuel tanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowyer_p._47_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowyer_p._47-142">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>Supermarine developed a two-seat variant known as the T Mk VIII to be used for <a href="/wiki/Flight_training" title="Flight training">training</a>, but none were ordered, and only one example was ever constructed (identified as N32/<i>G-AIDN</i> by Supermarine).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_2002.2C_p._224_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_2002.2C_p._224-143">[130]</a></sup> In the absence of an official two-seater variant, a number of airframes were crudely converted in the field. These included a <a href="/wiki/4_Squadron_SAAF" title="4 Squadron SAAF">4 Squadron SAAF</a> Mk VB in North Africa, where a second seat was fitted instead of the upper fuel tank in front of the cockpit, although it was not a dual-control aircraft and is thought to have been used as the squadron "run-about".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_2002.2C_p._223_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_2002.2C_p._223-144">[131]</a></sup> The only unofficial two-seat conversions that were fitted with dual-controls were a small number of Russian lend/lease Mk IX aircraft. These were referred to as Mk IX UTI and differed from the Supermarine proposals by using an inline "greenhouse" style double canopy rather than the raised "bubble" type of the T Mk VIII.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_2002.2C_p._223_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price_2002.2C_p._223-144">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the postwar era, the idea was revived by Supermarine and a number of two-seat Spitfires were built by converting old Mk IX airframes with a second "raised" cockpit featuring a <a href="/wiki/Bubble_canopy" title="Bubble canopy">bubble canopy</a>. Ten of these TR9 variants were then sold to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Air_Force" title="Indian Air Force">Indian Air Force</a> along with six to the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Air_Corps" title="Irish Air Corps">Irish Air Corps</a>, three to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Netherlands_Air_Force" title="Royal Netherlands Air Force">Royal Netherlands Air Force</a> and one for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Egyptian_Air_Force" title="Royal Egyptian Air Force">Royal Egyptian Air Force</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_2002.2C_p._224_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Price_2002.2C_p._224-143">[130]</a></sup> Currently several of the trainers are known to exist, including both the T Mk VIII, a T Mk IX based in the US, and the "Grace Spitfire" <i>ML407</i>, a veteran flown operationally by <a href="/wiki/No._485_Squadron_RNZAF" title="No. 485 Squadron RNZAF">485(NZ) Squadron</a> in 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grace_Spitfire_ML407._145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grace_Spitfire_ML407.-145">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[nb 14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seafire">Seafire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Seafire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HMS_Implacable_AWM_019037.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="568" data-file-width="765" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/HMS_Implacable_AWM_019037.jpg/220px-HMS_Implacable_AWM_019037.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/HMS_Implacable_AWM_019037.jpg/330px-HMS_Implacable_AWM_019037.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/HMS_Implacable_AWM_019037.jpg/440px-HMS_Implacable_AWM_019037.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HMS_Implacable_AWM_019037.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Seafires preparing to take off from the aircraft carrier <a href="/wiki/HMS_Implacable_(R86)" title="HMS Implacable (R86)">HMS <i>Implacable</i></a> in 1945</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Seafire" title="Supermarine Seafire">Supermarine Seafire</a></div>
<p>The Seafire, a name derived from <i>sea</i>, and <i>Spitfire</i>, was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval" title="Naval">naval</a> version of the Spitfire specially adapted for operation from <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>. Although the Spitfire was not designed for the rough-and-tumble of carrier-deck operations, it was considered to be the best available fighter at the time. The basic Spitfire design did impose some limitations on the use of the aircraft as a carrier-based fighter; poor visibility over the nose, for example, meant that pilots had to be trained to land with their heads out of the cockpit and looking alongside the port cowling of their Seafire;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[133]</a></sup> also, like the Spitfire, the Seafire had a relatively narrow undercarriage track, which meant that it was not ideally suited to deck operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_p._108_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_p._108-148">[134]</a></sup> Early marks of Seafire had relatively few modifications to the standard Spitfire airframe; however cumulative front line experience meant that most of the later versions of the Seafire had strengthened airframes, folding wings, arrestor hooks and other modifications, culminating in the purpose-built Seafire F/FR Mk 47.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Seafire II was able to outperform the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Zero" title="Mitsubishi Zero">A6M5 Zero</a> at low altitudes when the two types were tested against each other during wartime mock combat exercises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_WoF_pt2.2C_p._40_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_WoF_pt2.2C_p._40-150">[136]</a></sup> However, contemporary Allied carrier fighters such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F6F_Hellcat" title="F6F Hellcat">F6F Hellcat</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F4U_Corsair" title="F4U Corsair">F4U Corsair</a> were considerably more robust and so more practical for carrier operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_WoF_pt_2.2C_p._36_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_WoF_pt_2.2C_p._36-151">[137]</a></sup> Performance was greatly increased when later versions of the Seafire were fitted with the Griffon engines. These were too late to see service in World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andrews.26Morgan_pp._255.E2.80.936_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andrews.26Morgan_pp._255.E2.80.936-152">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Griffon-engined_variants">Griffon-engined variants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Griffon-engined variants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_(Griffon-powered_variants)" title="Supermarine Spitfire (Griffon-powered variants)">Supermarine Spitfire (Griffon-powered variants)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_IV_XII_DP845.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="331" data-file-width="500" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Spitfire_IV_XII_DP845.jpg/220px-Spitfire_IV_XII_DP845.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Spitfire_IV_XII_DP845.jpg/330px-Spitfire_IV_XII_DP845.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Spitfire_IV_XII_DP845.jpg/440px-Spitfire_IV_XII_DP845.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_IV_XII_DP845.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first Griffon-powered Spitfire, <i>DP845</i>, flown by Jeffrey Quill, 1942 (Mk XII)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rolls_Royce_Griffon" title="Rolls Royce Griffon">Rolls Royce Griffon</a>-engined Mk XII flew in August 1942, and first flew operationally with <a href="/wiki/No._41_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 41 Squadron RAF">41 Squadron</a> in April 1943. This mark could nudge 400 mph (640 km/h) in level flight and climb to an altitude of 33,000 ft (10,000 m) in under nine minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>As American fighters took over the long-range escorting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USAAF" title="USAAF">USAAF</a> daylight bombing raids, the Griffon-engined Spitfires progressively took up the tactical air superiority role, and played a major role in intercepting <a href="/wiki/V-1_flying_bomb" title="V-1 flying bomb">V-1 flying bombs</a>, while the Merlin-engined variants (mainly the Mk IX and the Packard-engined Mk XVI) were adapted to the fighter-bomber role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[140]</a></sup> Although the later Griffon-engined marks lost some of the favourable handling characteristics of their Merlin-powered predecessors, they could still outmanoeuvre their main German foes and other, later American and British-designed fighters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowyer_p._47_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowyer_p._47-142">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>The final version of the Spitfire, the Mk 24, first flew at South Marston on 13 April 1946. On 20 February 1948, almost twelve years from the prototype's first flight, the last production Spitfire, <i>VN496</i>, left the production line. Spitfire Mk 24s were used by only one regular RAF unit, with <a href="/wiki/No._80_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 80 Squadron RAF">80 Squadron</a> replacing their <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Tempest" title="Hawker Tempest">Hawker Tempests</a> with F Mk 24s in 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[141]</a></sup> With these planes, 80 Squadron continued its patrol and reconnaissance duties from <a href="/wiki/Wunstorf" title="Wunstorf">Wunstorf</a> in Germany as part of the occupation forces, until it relocated to <a href="/wiki/Kai_Tak_Airport" title="Kai Tak Airport">Kai Tak Airport</a>, Hong Kong in July 1949. During the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a>, 80 Squadron's main duty was to defend Hong Kong from perceived Communist threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[142]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supermarine_Spitfire_F_Mk_XIIs_of_41_Sqn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="770" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Supermarine_Spitfire_F_Mk_XIIs_of_41_Sqn.jpg/220px-Supermarine_Spitfire_F_Mk_XIIs_of_41_Sqn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Supermarine_Spitfire_F_Mk_XIIs_of_41_Sqn.jpg/330px-Supermarine_Spitfire_F_Mk_XIIs_of_41_Sqn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Supermarine_Spitfire_F_Mk_XIIs_of_41_Sqn.jpg/440px-Supermarine_Spitfire_F_Mk_XIIs_of_41_Sqn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Supermarine_Spitfire_F_Mk_XIIs_of_41_Sqn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Supermarine Spitfire LF Mk XIIs of <a href="/wiki/No._41_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 41 Squadron RAF">41 Squadron</a> in April 1944</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Operation Firedog during the <a href="/wiki/Malayan_Emergency" title="Malayan Emergency">Malayan Emergency</a> saw the Spitfire fly over 1,800 operational sorties against the Malaysian Communists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deltaweb_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-deltaweb-157">[143]</a></sup> The last operational sortie of an RAF Spitfire was flown on 1 April 1954, by PR Mk 19 Spitfire <i>PS888</i> flying from <a href="/wiki/Seletar_Airport" title="Seletar Airport">RAF Seletar</a>, in Singapore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81squadron_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-81squadron-158">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>The last non-operational flight of a Spitfire in RAF service, which took place on 9 June 1957, was by a PR Mk 19, <i>PS583</i>, from <a href="/wiki/RAF_Woodvale" title="RAF Woodvale">RAF Woodvale</a> of the Temperature and Humidity Flight. This was also the last known flight of a piston-engined fighter in the RAF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[145]</a></sup> The last nation in the Middle East to operate Spitfires was Syria, which kept its F 22s until 1953.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deltaweb_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-deltaweb-157">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 1962, Air Marshal Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Nicholls_(RAF_officer)" title="John Nicholls (RAF officer)">John Nicholls</a> instigated a trial when he flew Spitfire <i>PM631</i>, a PR Mk 19 in the custody of the Battle of Britain Memorial Flight, against an <a href="/wiki/English_Electric_Lightning" title="English Electric Lightning">English Electric Lightning F 3</a> (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersonic" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> jet-engined interceptor) in mock combat at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Binbrook" title="RAF Binbrook">RAF Binbrook</a>. At the time British Commonwealth forces were involved in possible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indonesia-Malaysia_confrontation" title="Indonesia-Malaysia confrontation">action against Indonesia over Malaya</a> and Nicholls decided to develop tactics to fight the Indonesian Air Force P-51 Mustang, a fighter that had a similar performance to the PR Mk 19.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_p._91_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green_p._91-160">[146]</a></sup> The first airframe (PM631) developed mechanical issues which removed it from the trial. Another PR Mk 19, <i>PS853</i> which is now owned by Rolls-Royce, was on gate-guard duties at Binbrook, having been retired from the BBMF one year before. It had been maintained in running condition by ground crews at Binbrook, and after a short time was participating in the trials. At the end of the trials, RAF pilots found that Firestreak infra-red guided missiles had trouble acquiring the Spitfire due to a low exhaust temperature, and decided that the twin ADEN 30 mm (1 in) cannons were the only weapons suited to the task, which was complicated by the tight turning circle of the Spitfire and the Lightning's proclivity for over-running the Spitfire. It was concluded that the most effective and safest way for a modern jet-engined fighter to attack a piston-engined fighter was to engage full afterburner at an altitude lower than the Spitfire, and behind it to perform a hit-and-run attack, contrary to all established fighter-on-fighter doctrine at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._158_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._158-162">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operators">Operators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Operators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Surviving_aircraft">Surviving aircraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Surviving aircraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supermarine_Spitfire_FR_Mk.XVIIIe_SM845_RAF_Leuchars_2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Supermarine_Spitfire_FR_Mk.XVIIIe_SM845_RAF_Leuchars_2008.jpg/220px-Supermarine_Spitfire_FR_Mk.XVIIIe_SM845_RAF_Leuchars_2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Supermarine_Spitfire_FR_Mk.XVIIIe_SM845_RAF_Leuchars_2008.jpg/330px-Supermarine_Spitfire_FR_Mk.XVIIIe_SM845_RAF_Leuchars_2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Supermarine_Spitfire_FR_Mk.XVIIIe_SM845_RAF_Leuchars_2008.jpg/440px-Supermarine_Spitfire_FR_Mk.XVIIIe_SM845_RAF_Leuchars_2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Supermarine_Spitfire_FR_Mk.XVIIIe_SM845_RAF_Leuchars_2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Supermarine Spitfire FR Mk.XVIIIe SM845 RAF Leuchars' airshow in 2008</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitifre_Mk.XVI_with_crew.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Spitifre_Mk.XVI_with_crew.jpg/220px-Spitifre_Mk.XVI_with_crew.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Spitifre_Mk.XVI_with_crew.jpg/330px-Spitifre_Mk.XVI_with_crew.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Spitifre_Mk.XVI_with_crew.jpg/440px-Spitifre_Mk.XVI_with_crew.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitifre_Mk.XVI_with_crew.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spitfire XIVe <a href="/wiki/List_of_surviving_Supermarine_Spitfires" title="List of surviving Supermarine Spitfires"><i>NH749</i></a> of the <a href="/wiki/Commemorative_Air_Force" title="Commemorative Air Force">Commemorative Air Force</a>, based at <a href="/wiki/Camarillo,_California" title="Camarillo, California">Camarillo airport, Southern California</a>, seen with period-dressed crew members in 2011.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_surviving_Supermarine_Spitfires" title="List of surviving Supermarine Spitfires">List of surviving Supermarine Spitfires</a></div>
<p>There are approximately 55 Spitfires and a few Seafires in airworthy condition worldwide, although many air museums have examples on static display, for example, Chicago's <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Science_and_Industry_(Chicago)" title="Museum of Science and Industry (Chicago)">Museum of Science and Industry</a> has paired a static Spitfire with a static Ju 87 R-2/Trop. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stuka" title="Stuka">Stuka</a> dive bomber. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[nb 15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>The oldest surviving Spitfire is a Mark 1, serial number <i>K9942</i>; it is preserved at the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force_Museum_Cosford" title="Royal Air Force Museum Cosford">Royal Air Force Museum Cosford</a> in Shropshire. This aircraft was the 155th built and first flew in April 1939. It flew operationally with <a href="/wiki/No._72_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 72 Squadron RAF">No. 72 Squadron RAF</a> until June 1940, when it was damaged in a wheels-up landing. After repair, it was used for training until August 1944, when it became one of several <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a> aircraft veterans that were allocated to the Air Historical Branch for future museum preservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p>What may be the most originally restored Spitfire in the world is maintained in airworthy condition at <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_of_Flight" title="Fantasy of Flight">Fantasy of Flight</a> in Polk City, Florida. Over a six-year period in the 1990s, this aircraft was slowly restored by Personal Plane Services in England using almost 90% of its original aircraft skins. Owner <a href="/wiki/Kermit_Weeks" title="Kermit Weeks">Kermit Weeks</a> insisted that the aircraft be restored to original condition as closely as possible. Machine guns, cannon, gun sight and original working radios are all installed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fantasy_of_Flight_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fantasy_of_Flight-167">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two MK 1 Supermarine Spitfires originally restored remain in a flying condition at the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_War_Museum_Duxford" title="Imperial War Museum Duxford">Imperial War Museum Duxford</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire" title="Cambridgeshire">Cambridgeshire</a>, England. Both restored by American billionaire <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kaplan" title="Thomas Kaplan">Thomas Kaplan</a>, one has been donated to the Imperial War Museum and the second was auctioned in July 2015 at <a href="/wiki/Christie%27s" title="Christie's">Christie's</a>, London. Being one of only four flying MK 1 Spitfires in the world, the aircraft fetched a record £3.1 million at auction on 9 July, beating the previous record for a Spitfire of £1.7 million set in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surviving_Spitfires_in_Burma">Surviving Spitfires in Burma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Surviving Spitfires in Burma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After hostilities ceased in Asia in 1945, a number of Spitfire Mk.XIVs were reportedly buried, after being greased, tarred and prepared for long-term storage, in crates in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>.</p>
<p>Excavations carried out at <a href="/wiki/Yangon_International_Airport" title="Yangon International Airport">Yangon International Airport</a> (formerly RAF Mingaladon) in early 2013 failed to locate any of the rumoured aircraft<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[155]</a></sup> and the team reported that they found no evidence that Spitfires were shipped there in crates or buried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[156]</a></sup> Pat Woodward, who was an RAF pilot operating from Burma at the end of the war, reported that no such burying took place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is widely reported<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (April 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> from numerous sources that aircraft from RAF bases in Burma were actually crated up and shipped out for reuse in elsewhere.</p>
<p>In 2016 it was reported that the hunt was continuing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorials">Memorials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Memorials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>A fibreglass replica of the Mk.1 Spitfire Mk1 YT-J (R6675), flown by Supermarine test pilot <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Quill" title="Jeffrey Quill">Jeffrey Quill</a> during his brief period of active service with 65 Squadron is on display at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_Memorial,_Capel-le-Ferne" title="Battle of Britain Memorial, Capel-le-Ferne">Battle of Britain memorial</a> at <a href="/wiki/Capel-le-Ferne" title="Capel-le-Ferne">Capel-le-Ferne</a> near Folkestone, along with a replica Mk.1 Hurricane representing US-X, in which Pilot Officer <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Page" title="Geoffrey Page">Geoffrey Page</a> was shot down on 12 August 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[160]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Sentinel_(sculpture)" title="Sentinel (sculpture)">Sentinel</a></i> is a sculpture depicting three Spitfires in flight by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Tolkien" title="Tim Tolkien">Tim Tolkien</a> at the roundabout junction (popularly known as Spitfire Island) of the A47 and A452 in <a href="/wiki/Castle_Bromwich" title="Castle Bromwich">Castle Bromwich</a>, Birmingham England, commemorating the main Spitfire factory. The island sits at the adjoining southern corners of the former Castle Bromwich Aircraft Factory and Aerodrome (now Castle Vale housing estate).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[161]</a></sup> There is also both a Spitfire and a Hurricane in the nearby <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thinktank,_Birmingham" title="Thinktank, Birmingham">Thinktank</a> Science Museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[162]</a></sup></li>
<li>A sculpture of the prototype Spitfire, <i>K5054</i>, stands on the roundabout at the entrance to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southampton_International_Airport" title="Southampton International Airport">Southampton International Airport</a>, which, as Eastleigh Aerodrome, saw the first flight of the aircraft in March 1936.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Quill" title="Jeffrey Quill">Jeffrey Quill</a>, the former Supermarine test pilot, initiated a project to build an exact replica of K5054, the prototype Spitfire to be put on permanent public display as a memorial to R.J. Mitchell. A team of original Supermarine designers worked with Aerofab Restorations of Andover for 10 years to create the facsimile. It was unveiled to the public in April 1993 by Quill at the RAF Museum, Hendon, and is currently on loan to the Tangmere Military Aviation Museum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_2006.2C_p._206_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_2006.2C_p._206-178">[163]</a></sup></li>
<li>A fibreglass replica in the colours of a Polish Squadron Leader based at the station during the Second World War is on display at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Northolt" title="RAF Northolt">RAF Northolt</a>, the last <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a> Sector Station still in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAF" title="RAF">RAF</a> operational service.</li>
<li>A replica Spitfire is on display on the Thornaby Road roundabout near the school named after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Douglas_Bader" title="Sir Douglas Bader">Sir Douglas Bader</a> who flew a Spitfire in the Second World War. This memorial is in memory of the old <a href="/wiki/RAF_Thornaby" title="RAF Thornaby">RAF base in Thornaby</a> which is now a residential estate.</li>
<li>A fibreglass replica of a Spitfire has been mounted on a pylon in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memorial_Park,_Hamilton" title="Memorial Park, Hamilton">Memorial Park, Hamilton</a>, New Zealand as a tribute to all New Zealand fighter pilots who flew Spitfires during the Second World War.</li>
<li>At <a href="/wiki/RAF_Bentley_Priory" title="RAF Bentley Priory">Bentley Priory</a>, the Second World War command centre for Fighter Command, fibreglass replicas of a Spitfire Mk 1 and a Hurricane Mk 1 can be seen fixed in a position of attack. This was built as a memorial to everyone who worked at Bentley Priory during the war.</li>
<li>A fibreglass replica in the colours of 603 (City of <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>) Squadron Royal Auxiliary Air Force Spitfire Memorial next to the <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Airport" title="Edinburgh Airport">Edinburgh Airport</a> control tower. This model replaced the original <a href="/wiki/Gate_guardian" title="Gate guardian">gate guardian</a> from the former <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RAF_Turnhouse" title="RAF Turnhouse">RAF Turnhouse</a>. It is painted to represent serial number L1067 (code XT-D) "Blue Peter", the personal aircraft of the Squadron Commander, Squadron Leader <a href="/wiki/George_Denholm" title="George Denholm">George Denholm</a> DFC.</li>
<li>A fibreglass replica of a Spitfire Mk IX has been mounted on a pylon in Jackson Park, <a href="/wiki/Windsor,_Ontario" title="Windsor, Ontario">Windsor, Ontario</a> alongside a Hurricane as a memorial to <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Air_Force" title="Royal Canadian Air Force">Royal Canadian Air Force</a> pilots. This display replaces an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avro_Lancaster_bomber" title="Avro Lancaster bomber">Avro Lancaster bomber</a> that had previously been on display and is currently undergoing restoration.</li>
<li>One of the few remaining Supermarine Spitfires with a wartime record is on display (alongside a Hawker Hurricane) at the <a href="/wiki/RAF_Manston" title="RAF Manston">RAF Manston</a> Spitfire and Hurricane Memorial Museum, near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kent_International_Airport" title="Kent International Airport">Kent International Airport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lodge Hill Garage, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abingdon,_Oxfordshire" title="Abingdon, Oxfordshire">Abingdon, Oxfordshire</a> has a full-size replica Spitfire as its own rooftop monument. Owner Peter Jewson bought the replica in a campaign to build the first ever national memorial to honour the 166 women from the <a href="/wiki/Air_Transport_Auxiliary" title="Air Transport Auxiliary">Air Transport Auxiliary</a> (ATA) who flew Spitfires and other aircraft from factories to their operational airbases; 14 died during these ferry flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[165]</a></sup></li>
<li>A fibreglass replica of a Spitfire Mk IX is mounted to the roof of the speciality shop, Spitfire Emporium, in <a href="/wiki/Kitchener,_Ontario" title="Kitchener, Ontario">Kitchener, Ontario</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[166]</a></sup></li>
<li>There is a replica of a Spitfire (and of a Hurricane) at the entrance to the <a href="/wiki/Eden_Camp_Museum" title="Eden Camp Museum">Eden Camp Modern History Museum</a> as a memorial to pilots who served in the Battle of Britain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[167]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Montrose_Air_Station_Heritage_Centre" title="Montrose Air Station Heritage Centre">Montrose Air Station Heritage Centre</a> has a full size replica Spitfire MkVb LO-D (EP121) on display as a memorial to the men and women who served at RFC/RAF Montrose.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Replicas">Replicas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Replicas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpitfireVB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="980" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/SpitfireVB.jpg/220px-SpitfireVB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/SpitfireVB.jpg/330px-SpitfireVB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/SpitfireVB.jpg/440px-SpitfireVB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpitfireVB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Replica Mk Vb on display in 2009</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several small manufacturers have produced replica Spitfires, either as complete aircraft, or as kits for self-building. These range in scale from ¾ full scale to full-size, although most use wooden construction, rather than the original all-metal monocoque design.</p>
<p>The British <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Historic_Flying_Company&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Historic Flying Company (page does not exist)">Historic Flying Company</a> has either restored or built from scratch a significant proportion of the Spitfires that are now air-worthy. Other examples include the <a href="/wiki/Jurca_Spit" title="Jurca Spit">Jurca Spit</a> from France, and those manufactured by Tally Ho Enterprises in Canada,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[168]</a></sup> SAC in California, USA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[169]</a></sup> and even the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microlight" title="Microlight">microlight</a> Silence Twister from Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[170]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Aircraft" title="Supermarine Aircraft">Supermarine Aircraft</a> originally from <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a>, Australia, and now based in Cisco <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>, manufacture the 80% scale Spitfire Mk 26 and the 90% scale Mk 26B replicas. The <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_Mk_26" title="Supermarine Spitfire Mk 26">Supermarine Spitfire Mk 26</a> and 26B are supplied in kit form and are the only all-aluminium reproduction Spitfires in production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Supermarine_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Supermarine-186">[171]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Isaacs_Spitfire" title="Isaacs Spitfire">Isaacs Spitfire</a> is a homebuilt 60% scale replica.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearances_in_media">Appearances in media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Appearances in media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During and after the Battle of Britain the Spitfire became a symbol of British resistance: for example, <a href="/wiki/Max_Aitken,_1st_Baron_Beaverbrook" title="Max Aitken, 1st Baron Beaverbrook">Lord Beaverbrook's</a> "Spitfire Fund" of 1940 was one campaign which drew widespread public attention to the Spitfire. The Spitfire continues to be highly popular at airshows, on airfields and in museums worldwide, and continues to hold an important place in the memories of many people, especially the few still living who flew the Spitfire in combat. Numerous films and documentaries featuring the Spitfire are still being produced, some of which are listed in this section.</p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_First_of_the_Few" title="The First of the Few">The First of the Few</a></i> (also known as <i>Spitfire</i> in the US and Canada) (1942) was a British film produced and directed by <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Howard_(actor)" title="Leslie Howard (actor)">Leslie Howard</a>, with Howard in the starring role of R.J. Mitchell, and David Niven playing a composite character based on the Schneider Trophy pilots of 1927, 1929 and 1931, and the Supermarine test pilot <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Quill" title="Jeffrey Quill">Jeffrey Quill</a>. Some of the footage includes film shot in 1941 of operational Spitfires and pilots of <a href="/wiki/No._501_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 501 Squadron RAF">501 Squadron</a> (code letters SD). Howard spent a long time researching the history of the Spitfire's development for the film; Mrs. Mitchell and her son Gordon were on the set during much of the production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[172]</a></sup> The aerobatic flying sequences featured in the last 15 minutes of the film were made by Jeffrey Quill in early November 1941, flying a Spitfire Mk II mocked up to represent the prototype.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Malta_Story" title="Malta Story">Malta Story</a></i> (1953), starring <a href="/wiki/Alec_Guinness" title="Alec Guinness">Alec Guinness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Hawkins" title="Jack Hawkins">Jack Hawkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Steel_(actor)" title="Anthony Steel (actor)">Anthony Steel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muriel_Pavlow" title="Muriel Pavlow">Muriel Pavlow</a>, is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black-and-white" title="Black-and-white">black and white</a> <a href="/wiki/War_film" title="War film">war film</a> telling the story of the defence of <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> in 1942 when Spitfires were the island's main defence from air attacks.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Reach_for_the_Sky" title="Reach for the Sky">Reach for the Sky</a></i> (1956) starring <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_More" title="Kenneth More">Kenneth More</a> tells the story of <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Bader" title="Douglas Bader">Douglas Bader</a>, using contemporary Spitfire aircraft in the production.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain_(film)" title="Battle of Britain (film)">Battle of Britain</a></i> (1969) directed by <a href="/wiki/Guy_Hamilton" title="Guy Hamilton">Guy Hamilton</a> and starring <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Olivier" title="Laurence Olivier">Laurence Olivier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Caine" title="Michael Caine">Michael Caine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Plummer" title="Christopher Plummer">Christopher Plummer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Richardson" title="Ralph Richardson">Ralph Richardson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Redgrave" title="Michael Redgrave">Michael Redgrave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susannah_York" title="Susannah York">Susannah York</a> and many others. Set in 1940, this film features several sequences involving a total of 12 flying Spitfires (mostly Mk IX versions), as well as a number of other flying examples of Second World War-era British and German aircraft.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Eagles_Over_London" title="Eagles Over London">Eagles Over London</a></i> (1969), a wholly fictional Italian film, features Spitfires in a recreation of the Battle of Britain.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Piece_of_Cake_(TV_series)" title="Piece of Cake (TV series)">Piece of Cake</a></i> (1987) starring <a href="/wiki/Tom_Burlinson" title="Tom Burlinson">Tom Burlinson</a>. Aired on the ITV network in 1987. Based on the novel by Derek Robinson, the six-part miniseries covered the prewar era to "Battle of Britain Day," 15 September 1940. The series depicted air combat over the skies of France and Britain during the early stages of the Second World War, though using five flying examples of late model Spitfires in place of the novel's early model Hurricanes. There were shots of Spitfires taking off and landing together from grass airstrips.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Dark_Blue_World" title="Dark Blue World">Dark Blue World</a></i> (2001), starring <a href="/wiki/Ond%C5%99ej_Vetch%C3%BD" title="Ondřej Vetchý">Ondřej Vetchý</a> was a tale of two Czech pilots who escape Nazi-occupied Europe to fly Spitfires during the Battle of Britain. <a href="/wiki/Jan_Sv%C4%9Br%C3%A1k" title="Jan Svěrák">Jan Svěrák</a> filmed some new aerial scenes and reused aerial footage from Hamilton's film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_2006.2C_p._201_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_2006.2C_p._201-188">[173]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/James_May%27s_Toy_Stories" title="James May's Toy Stories">James May's Toy Stories</a></i> (2009), starring <a href="/wiki/James_May" title="James May">James May</a> was a BBC TV series which featured an episode in which children constructed a 1:1 scale model of the Spitfire in the style of the <a href="/wiki/Airfix" title="Airfix">Airfix</a> 1/72 scale model first released in 1953.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">Doctor Who</a></i> – "<a href="/wiki/Victory_of_the_Daleks" title="Victory of the Daleks">Victory of the Daleks</a>" (2010), was an episode of a popular BBC TV series in which three Spitfires modified for spaceflight aid in defending London from alien <a href="/wiki/Dalek" title="Dalek">Daleks</a> during the Blitz.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Inform-Educate-Entertain" title="Inform-Educate-Entertain">Inform-Educate-Entertain</a></i> (2013), the debut album by alternative British band <a href="/wiki/Public_Service_Broadcasting_(band)" title="Public Service Broadcasting (band)">Public Service Broadcasting (band)</a> features the single 'Spitfire' in which the design and construction of the plane are described through various samples to the backing of the song itself.</li>
<li><i>Guy Martin's Spitfire</i> (2014) was a Channel 4 documentary covering the two-year restoration of a Mark 1 Spitfire, N3200, coded 'QV', that had been buried beneath the sand for 46 years after crash landing on a French beach during the Dunkirk evacuation in 1940. <a href="/wiki/Guy_Martin" title="Guy Martin">Guy Martin</a> tells the Boy's Own-style story of its pilot, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_D._Stephenson" title="Geoffrey D. Stephenson">Squadron Leader Geoffrey Stephenson</a> and helps in the restoration of the aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[174]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specifications_.28Spitfire_Mk_VB.29">Specifications (Spitfire Mk VB)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Specifications (Spitfire Mk VB)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Spitfire's performance improved greatly as WWII progressed, for more information see <a href="/wiki/Supermarine_Spitfire_variants:_specifications,_performance_and_armament" title="Supermarine Spitfire variants: specifications, performance and armament">Supermarine Spitfire variants: specifications, performance and armament</a>.</p>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supermarine_Spitfire_3D_ExCC.gif"><img alt="Supermarine Spitfire 3D ExCC.gif" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="650" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Supermarine_Spitfire_3D_ExCC.gif/300px-Supermarine_Spitfire_3D_ExCC.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Supermarine_Spitfire_3D_ExCC.gif/450px-Supermarine_Spitfire_3D_ExCC.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Supermarine_Spitfire_3D_ExCC.gif/600px-Supermarine_Spitfire_3D_ExCC.gif 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_VB_222_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="655" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Spitfire_VB_222_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg/220px-Spitfire_VB_222_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Spitfire_VB_222_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg/330px-Spitfire_VB_222_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Spitfire_VB_222_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg/440px-Spitfire_VB_222_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spitfire_VB_222_Sqn_RAF_in_flight_1942.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1942 colour photograph of a Spitfire VB of <a href="/wiki/No._222_Squadron_RAF" title="No. 222 Squadron RAF">222 Squadron</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p style="font-size: 90%; margin:0; padding: .2em"><i>Data from</i> <i>Spitfire: The History</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[175]</a></sup> and <i>Jane's Fighting Aircraft of World War II</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jane_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-jane-191">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>General characteristics</b></span></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Crew:</b> one pilot</li>
<li><b>Length:</b> 29 ft 11 in (9.12 m)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wingspan" title="Wingspan">Wingspan</a>:</b> 36 ft 10 in (11.23 m)</li>
<li><b>Height:</b> 11 ft 5 in (3.86 m)</li>
<li><b>Wing area:</b> 242.1 ft<sup>2</sup> (22.48 m<sup>2</sup>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Airfoil" title="Airfoil">Airfoil</a>:</b> NACA 2213 (root)<br/>
NACA 2209.4 (tip)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Manufacturer%27s_empty_weight" title="Manufacturer's empty weight">Empty weight</a>:</b> 5,065 <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">lb</a> (2,297 kg)</li>
<li><b>Loaded weight:</b> 6,622 lb (3,000 kg)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Maximum_takeoff_weight" title="Maximum takeoff weight">Max. takeoff weight</a>:</b> 6,700 lb (3,039 kg)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Aircraft_engine" title="Aircraft engine">Powerplant</a>:</b> 1 × <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Merlin" title="Rolls-Royce Merlin">Rolls-Royce Merlin 45</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[nb 16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Supercharger" title="Supercharger">supercharged</a> <a href="/wiki/V12_engine" title="V12 engine">V12 engine</a>, 1,470 hp (1,096 kW) at 9,250 ft (2,819 m)</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>Performance</b></span></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/V_speeds#Regulatory_V-speeds" title="V speeds">Maximum speed</a>:</b> 370 mph (322 <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">kn</a>, 595 km/h)</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combat_radius" title="Combat radius">Combat radius</a>:</b> 410 <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nmi</a> (470 mi (756 km))</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Range_(aeronautics)" title="Range (aeronautics)">Ferry range</a>:</b> 991 nmi (1,135 mi (1,827 km))</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Ceiling_(aeronautics)" title="Ceiling (aeronautics)">Service ceiling</a>:</b> 36,500 ft (11,125 m)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Rate_of_climb" title="Rate of climb">Rate of climb</a>:</b> 2,600 ft/min (13.2 m/s)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Wing_loading" title="Wing loading">Wing loading</a>:</b> 27.35 lb/ft<sup>2</sup> (133.5 kg/m<sup>2</sup>)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Power-to-weight_ratio" title="Power-to-weight ratio">Power/mass</a>:</b> 0.22 <a href="/wiki/Horsepower" title="Horsepower">hp</a>/lb (0.36 kW/kg)</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="font-size: 120%;"><b>Armament</b></span><br/></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Guns:</b>
<ul>
<li>A wing
<ul>
<li>8 × .303 in Browning Mk II* machine guns (350 rounds per gun)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>B wing
<ul>
<li>2 × 20mm Hispano Mk II (60 rounds per gun)</li>
<li>4 × .303 in Browning Mk II* machine guns (350 rounds per gun)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>C wing
<ul>
<li>4 × 20mm Hispano Mk II cannon (120 rounds per gun)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>C wing (Alt.)
<ul>
<li>2 × 20mm Hispano Mk II (120 rounds per gun)</li>
<li>4 × .303 in Browning Mk II* machine guns (350 rounds per gun)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>E wing
<ul>
<li>2 × 20mm Hispano Mk II cannon (120 rounds per gun)</li>
<li>2 × .50 in M2 Browning machine guns (250 rounds per gun)</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supermarine_Spitfire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Unit cost for airframe complete with engine, armament and equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Price_p._67_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Price_p._67-4">[4]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For many years there was considerable debate over the date of the first flight being the 5 or 6 March. Many of Supermarine's records from this era were destroyed during a bombing raid during 1940, and none of the surviving documents seemed to pin this down. This matter was almost conclusively answered in 1985 by aviation author Alfred Price, who received an account sheet with a hand written note written by Mitchell updating a line from "Not yet flown" to "Flew 5 Mar 36". See <i>Spitfire: A Complete Fighting History</i>, 1991, p. 165-166.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although this is often perceived as Summers implying the Spitfire was flawless, this is an error. What he meant was that he wanted nothing touched, especially the control settings, until he had consulted with Mitchell and the design team and suggested some improvements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Air Ministry submitted a list of possible names to Vickers-Armstrongs for the new aircraft, then known as the Type 300. One of these was the improbable <i>Shrew</i>. The name <i>Spitfire</i> was suggested by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Robert_McLean" title="Sir Robert McLean">Sir Robert McLean</a>, director of Vickers-Armstrongs at the time, who called his spirited elder daughter Annie Penrose "a little spitfire".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> The word dates from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabethan" title="Elizabethan">Elizabethan</a> times and refers to a fiery, ferocious type of person; at the time it usually meant a girl or woman of that temperament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> The name had previously been used unofficially for Mitchell's earlier F7/30 Type 224 design. Mitchell is reported to have said it was "just the sort of bloody silly name they would choose".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._55._24-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKinstry_2007.2C_p._55.-24">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The pilot standing in front of the aircraft is pre-War Olympic hurdler, Sqn. Ldr. Donald O. Finlay, the Commanding Officer of 41 Squadron from September 1940 – August 1941, who adopted the aircraft as his personal mount. The same day P7666 was delivered to the Squadron, 23 November 1940, Finlay destroyed a Bf 109 on his first operational sortie in the aircraft.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A "Spitfire Lane" can be found on the road between Salisbury and <a href="/wiki/Andover,_Hampshire" title="Andover, Hampshire">Andover</a> leading to the Chattis Hill aerodrome.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The test pilots were based at Highpost and flown by light aircraft to the other airfields.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Glancey notes that Rolls-Royce saw the potential of the He 70 as a flying test-bed for prototype engines, sending a team to Germany to buy one of the aircraft direct from Heinkel. The German government approved the deal, but only in return for a number of <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Kestrel" title="Rolls-Royce Kestrel">Rolls-Royce Kestrel</a> engines. He also notes that Shenstone had worked with <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Heinkel" title="Ernst Heinkel">Ernst Heinkel</a> in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[60]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Starting with the Merlin XII fitted in Spitfire Mk IIs in late 1940 this was changed to a 70% water-30% glycol mix.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The fabric used for aircraft control surfaces had to be as light and as strong as possible: <a href="/wiki/Irish_linen" title="Irish linen">Irish linen</a> was often used, or Grade A cotton. Once the material was stretched and doped, it was weatherproof and aerodynamically smooth. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[71]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On the ground the flaps were normally lowered only for inspection or for maintenance. Pilots who forgot to raise the flaps after landing often found themselves paying a fine.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Early, Merlin-powered Spitfires were not the only aircraft to suffer from this problem as other pre-war aeroplanes also used carburettors containing a float chamber. In 1942, the United States acquired a flyable, Japanese <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_A6M_Zero" title="Mitsubishi A6M Zero">Mitsubishi A6M Zero</a>, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Akutan_Zero" title="Akutan Zero">Akutan Zero</a></i>. Test flying showed this aircraft also suffered engine cut-outs, due to the float-type carburettor of its <i><a href="/wiki/Nakajima_Sakae" title="Nakajima Sakae">Nakajima Sakae</a></i> engine. The Americans devised tactics to counter the Zero in combat that took advantage of this and other results of testing the aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[93]</a></sup> .</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-111">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This aircraft survived the war, only to be scrapped in 1945. The first pilot to fly <i>K9789</i> was Squadron Leader Henry Cozens, whose career had begun in 1917 with the <a href="/wiki/Sopwith_Camel" title="Sopwith Camel">Sopwith Camel</a> and ended after flying <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Meteor" title="Gloster Meteor">Meteor</a> and <a href="/wiki/De_Havilland_Vampire" title="De Havilland Vampire">Vampire</a> jets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glancey_2006.2C_p._61_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glancey_2006.2C_p._61-35">[31]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The second cockpit of this aircraft has been lowered and is now below the front cockpit. This modification is known as the Grace Canopy Conversion, and was designed by Nick Grace, who rebuilt ML407.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grace_Spitfire_ML407._145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grace_Spitfire_ML407.-145">[132]</a></sup> (For further details on surviving Spitfires see <a href="/wiki/List_of_surviving_Supermarine_Spitfires" title="List of surviving Supermarine Spitfires">List of surviving Supermarine Spitfires</a>).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-164"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-164">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Both of these airframes has a significant history in that they were acquired in the Second World War and used in the first war drives, which preceded the US entry into the conflict. The Spitfire, donated by the British government in 1940, was the first example to come to the United States, and was used extensively as a propaganda tool, alongside the Stuka, recovered from the Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chicago_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chicago-163">[149]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-192"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-192">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Merlin 46 and Merlin 50 were also used in the VB.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
